Abstract
In this thesis, crack growth in hydrodynamic bearing mounted in large marine diesel engines
is investigated. This problem is of huge interest to the engine manufactures as the design
of bearings primarily consists of empirical data as only limited theoretical research exist.
A better understanding of the crack phenomena is key to improve bearing design in order
to increase life time of the bearings. Increased life time of the bearings will both lead to
a more reliable engine, but also an economical gain as the replacement of bearings is both
costly and time consuming.
The analysis of this problem is rather complex as hydrodynamic bearings consist of a lay-
ered material system. In additions to the complexity of the bearing itself, a hydrodynamic
bearing is also exposed to multiple load situations. The loading situations investigated
consisted of a normal load acting on the surface of the bearing, a pressure on the crack
faces and a shear force on the bearing surface.
The analysis of the bearing is divided into two parts. The ﬁrst part is analysing the
loading situations acting on the bearing in order to map the dominating factors contributing
to crack growth. The second part is related to crack propagation determining the path of
the crack related to a given loading situation. The goal of the second analysis is to relate
speciﬁc load situation to crack patterns in order to interpret the cause a crack when failed
bearings are examined.
The analysis is based on a 2D ﬁnite element model. The ﬁnite element model include
both elastic and elastic-plastic material behaviour. The result of the ﬁnite element model
is post processed in order to determine the energy release rate by utilizing the J-integral.
Only elastic studies has been conducted as the elastic-plastic model never came to work
due to a numerical issue. The crack propagation study is conducted by determining stress
intensity factors by utilizing an interaction integral. The stress intensity factors is then
utilized for predicting the crack path.
Based on the analysis of the damage each load situation contribute with, it was found
that the most damaging factor is the pressure acting on the crack faces.
However the
pressure acting inside the crack is depending on the length of the crack. Studies revealed
that the damage from the pressure inside the crack is very limited for a short crack, but
as the crack grow, the damaging eﬀect increases rapidly. The crack path study revealed
that the cracks generally grow straight down or at an angle in the direction of the shear
force applied on the surface. It was found that the magnitude of the shear force could be
connected to the inclination of the cracks. For signiﬁcant magnitudes of shear, the crack
grew just under the surface, and for minor amounts of shear the crack grow to and later
along the interface.
Keywords
Hydrodynamic bearing · Domain integrals · Energy release rate · Stress in-
tensity factors · Crack propagation path

Preface
This report represents the work of a master thesis conducted at the Technical University
of Denmark. The thesis "Fracture Toughness and Crack Propagation in Layered Material
Systems" documents the work carried out in a 32.5 ECTS points project extending over the
period January 2015 to July 2015.
The aim of the project is to provide marine engine manufacturer MAN Diesel & Turbo with
knowledge of the crack phenomena taking place in the hydrodynamic engine bearing. The
goal it to acquire knowledge which can improve the design of the bearing increasing its life
time and reliability.
The numerical models developed during the project has been conducted with guidance
from main supervisor Kim Lau Nielsen.
The experiment data has been provided from
MAN Diesel & Turbo together with guidance from experienced engineers in the ﬁeld.
Lastly a great thank to Professor in tribology Peder Klit for providing knowledge regarding
hydrodynamic bearing. A thanks to the supervisors Kim Lau Nielsen and Anders Voelund
for the contribution to the project, guidance and collaboration.
Kgs. Lyngby, July 2015
Kristian Juul

1 Introduction
In the latter years the requirement to output and eﬃciency of the large marine diesel engines
has increased. This has resulted in an increased load on the components of the engines.
Especially the load on the hydrodynamic bearing has increased causing failure due to crack
growth. In this project, the crack related failure appearing in the hydrodynamic bearings
will be investigated. The investigation of the bearing failure phenomena is of large interest
to the engine manufactures as the replacing of bearings or unpredicted bearing failure can
be very expensive for both the company and its customers. In general a longer lifetime of
the bearings would also increase the company proﬁt. To give a better understanding of the
problem a brief introduction to hydrodynamic bearings in term of how they work and what
they are made of is given.
Hydrodynamic bearings is used several places in the engine as presented in Figure 1.3a.
The advantage of a hydrodynamic bearing is its very high load carrying capacity, which
is needed to support the immense loads in an engine of this size. Basically there is two
types of hydrodynamic bearing in large marine diesel engines, which is thrust bearings and
journal bearings (see Figure 1.1). The diﬀerence between a thrust bearing and a journal
bearing is that thrust bearings are used for taking up axial loads and journal bearings are
used for taking up radial loads. Both bearings are slider bearings which means they create
their load carrying capacity based on the relative motion between two surfaces.
Between the two surfaces moving relative to each other is a ﬂuid medium (lubricant),
which is the actual source of the load capacity. The load capacity is created by a hydrody-
(a) Hydrodynamic thrust bearing.
(b) Hydrodynamic journal bearing.
Figure 1.1: The two types of hydrodynamic bearings, creating their load carrying capacity from
the wedge principle.
1
==PAGE:14==
2
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
namic wedge or physical wedge pressure-generating mechanism (basically the same principle
as water skiing where the inclination of the ski relative to the water surface create an up-
ward force). The principle is illustrated in Figure 1.2. By tilting one of the surfaces relative
to the other, the gap between the bearing surfaces is narrowing down. Assuming that the
relative velocity is suﬃciently high, the gap which is narrowing down will try to compress
the oil in order to squeeze it in between the two surfaces. The compression of the oil cre-
ates a pressure which will act on the bearing surfaces, separating the surfaces and thereby
lifting the load. When the bearing surfaces are separated, only very low friction is present,
which originate solely from shearing the oil (internal friction). The shear force will act on
the bearing surface, and is depending on the velocity gradient and the viscosity of the oil.
This means that ideally the only factors having inﬂuence on the shear is the viscosity and
velocity gradient. However in an actual bearing, particles in the oil get jammed between
bearing surfaces and the bearings surfaces themselves might come into contact creating a
signiﬁcant amount of shear. To sum up the loads in the bearing, a pressure acting normal to
the surface as illustrated in Figure 1.2 exist and a shear force which is both due to internal
friction in the lubricant and metallic contact exists. As mentioned the failure problem is
due to cracks in the bearing. Having a lubricant under pressure, will make it possible to
transfer forces onto the crack face as the pressurized lubricant can enter the cracks causing
additional damage. The pressure on crack faces is the third load type existing in the bearing
which is taken into consideration in this project. The situation of the load being carried
from one bearing surface to the other by a lubricant is generally an excellent property as it
reduces wear, but it also has drawbacks. A disadvantage of hydrodynamic bearings is that
a certain relative velocity is required in order to maintain the oil ﬁlm. When the engine is
not running, the shaft will sink down on the bearing creating a metallic contact. Thereby
for every start and stop of the engine, severer contact pressure and shear force is acting
on the surfaces. In order to avoid the shaft will cold welding to the bearing (due to high
contact pressure, when the engine is not running), a lining is applied to the surface of the
bearing. The lining typically consist of white metal (also called babbitt) applied to a much
stiﬀer steel backing. Babbitt is a soft metal which is easily damaged suggesting that is
might be unsuitable for a bearing surface subjected to large forces. However, its structure
consists of small hard crystals dispersed in a softer material (normally tin) which makes it
a metal matrix composite. The soft babbitt material actually has excellent properties as
it can be smeared out in case of damages (it is self repairing to some extent) and if the
lubricant is missing for a shorter period of time, the tin is melting acting as a lubricant.
Another feature is that the tin erodes to some extent between the harder particles creating
paths for the lubricant.
As the general principle of hydrodynamic bearings has now been explained, the crack
problem observed in the bearings will be introduced, which is the main focus of this project.
The phenomena will be explained based on observations and the theoretical/experimental
work performed in the ﬁeld. Lastly an introduction to the work and aim on this project is
given.
Surface cracks have been observed to appear in the bearing lining and is regarded as a
fatigue induced problem. As small cracks is observed from visual inspection, these cracks
continue to grow until they are suﬃciently large for ﬂakes of the lining to fall oﬀthe surface.
When the lining start falling oﬀ, the load carrying capacity created by the mechanism
previously explained is heavily reduced and might not be able to support the load. This
causes metallic contact as the oil ﬁlm cannot be maintained which lead to bearing failure
at an exponential rate. This is a large problem as the engine itself might sustain damage
from running with a failed bearing, ruining not only the bearing but also the engine. The
general hypothesis of the crack phenomena in the bearing is presented in Figure 1.3b, where
==PAGE:15==
3
Figure 1.2: Hydrodynamic wedge principle, pressure distribution and velocity proﬁle for bearing
in 2D (Jespersen [1]).
it is seen that the cracks start at the surface and then grow toward the interface. When the
interface is reached, the crack will deﬂect in order to avoid growing into the much stiﬀer
backing material. The crack will then grow along the interface until a ﬂake is suﬃciently
large to break oﬀ. Cracks initiating in the interface has also been observed, but is primarily
a result poor bonding between the lining and backing material, however this is diﬃcult to
investigate experimentally as the crack cannot be detected.
It should be noted that the mechanism in Figure 1.3b is a hypothesis and currently the
cause of the bearing failure and the mechanisms which has inﬂuence on the crack growth
is not fully mapped. Diﬀerent hypothesis has been established and experiments has been
conducted, but so far the phenomena is still only partially explained. The theoretical and
experimental work in the ﬁelds will now be presented.
Experimental Background
Several experiments has been performed regarding general fracture mechanics and fatigue
testing. The experiments has the common goal of being designed for theoretical ﬁtting.
The design criteria has been to keep the models as simple as possible and with as few
variables as possible.
However the eﬀect of the loading from the oil ﬁlm is diﬃcult to
simplify, since the theory of ﬂuid mechanics and the load carrying oil ﬁlm is depending on
several variables which cannot be decoupled. The experimental results are rather limited
within this ﬁeld of oil pressure induced fatigue/crack propagation. Amongst the studies
which was conducted is the work of Gyde [3], Halsteen [2] and Juul [4]. The idea behind
the studies was to produce a small scale bearing similar to the bearings in the engines and
thereby maintaining similar loading conditions. Under the experiments the parameters:
temperature, loading and friction was measured to the extend that was possible. Under
the experiments performed by Gyde [3], it was managed to replicate the fatigue damage,
which was observed in the actual bearings. An example of fatigue damage seen in bearings
is presented in Figure 1.4.
==PAGE:16==
4
CHAPTER 1. INTRODUCTION
(a)
(b)
Figure 1.3: (a):Location of hydrodynamic bearing in ship engine. (b):Example of bearing failure
due to fatigue Halsteen [2].
Figure 1.4: Cross sectional cut of fatigue crack approaching the interface (Juul [4]).
In addition to the hydrodynamic test rig conducting tests under realistic conditions,
a more conventional way of testing the material was also conducted.
This consists of
an ordinary pull test machine subjecting the lining material to a cyclic load, enabling a
comparison of fatigue properties to be conducted. However this type of test does not take
the speciﬁc load types into consideration. Also the lining material is tested without the
==PAGE:17==
5
backing material meaning the eﬀects of a layered material is not accounted for.
Gyde has also investigated the inﬂuence of the temperature on the fatigue strength.
The tests were performed in the span of temperatures from 65◦C to 145◦C. The experi-
ments showed that an increase in temperature signiﬁcantly reduced the fatigue strength.
Especially when the temperature exceeded 100◦C, the fatigue strength was severely aﬀected.
Theoretical Background
The main focus of the existing theoretical literature which exist is on the eﬀect of the oil
pressure induced fatigue, attempting to determine the life time. Several suggestions to what
causes the fatigue damage and crack propagation has been made, however none of these has
deﬁnitely proven correct. Amongst these postulates some are more popularly and reused
under other circumstances. One postulate is that the crack is being forces open in a load
cycle, allowing the oil to penetrate the crack. When the load is then removed, the crack is
closing again, however an amount of oil will be trapped inside the crack causing an internal
pressure resulting in crack propagation. Another postulate is based on the eﬀect of the
layered material (lining and backing material) on the stress situation. When the crack is
propagating, the stress intensity is reaching a critical point related to the material. It has
been shown that when two material with diﬀerent material properties relating to stiﬀness
and strength is used in a layered material, the stress intensity is aﬀected (Halsteen [2]). In
the case where the crack initiates in the stiﬀer of the two materials, the stress intensity is
higher than for a uniform material. As the crack is propagating toward the interface, the
stress intensity would rapidly increase and the interface acts as an ampliﬁer. In the opposite
case where the crack initiate in the softer material, the stress intensity would be lower than
expected and the interface acts as a damper, making the crack deﬂect and propagate along
the interface, however still in the softer material.
Current Study in Short
As an introduction to hydrodynamic bearings, the failure mechanisms and the work con-
ducted so far in the ﬁeld has now been given, an introduction to the study performed in
this project will be presented. This study is based on the investigation of the energy release
rate and crack propagation path related to diﬀerent load cases/load cycles. The goal is to
map which loading situations in the bearing is the most probable reason for the damage
observed in the bearings and obtain a better understanding of the crack phenomena in the
bearings. By achieving a better understanding of the phenomena, better prerequisite for
optimizing the bearings in terms of reducing crack growth is obtained. The goal of the crack
propagation path study is to connect distinct crack patterns to speciﬁc loading situations.
If this is possible, it is of large interest as cracks in failed bearings can be connected to
speciﬁc mechanisms which will help determine the reason for the failure in the given case.
This is important since multiple failure mechanism exist and when replacing a bearing in
an engine, the knowledge of why the bearing failed is important, so the problem can be
rectiﬁed preventing it from happening again. This study does not focus on the actual life-
time of the bearing (fatigue), but rather which actions are most probable to reduce the
lifetime and be the reason for failure. As mentioned other studies conducted within this
area, has been more focused on the actual fatigue crack growth, determining the lifetime
of the bearing, applying models as for example Paris law.
==PAGE:18==
==PAGE:19==
2 Problem Description
The purpose of this project is to acquire knowledge about the fracture toughness and crack
propagation in layered material systems under various loading conditions.
The layered
material system in this project speciﬁcally relates to hydrodynamic bearings used in large
marine diesel engines. These bearings typically consists of a soft ﬁlm layer (the lining)
and a much stiﬀer substrate (backing).
The layered material system will be modelled
as a functionally graded material (FGM), describing the transition from the ﬁlm to the
substrate with a diﬀerentiable function. The project will analyse loading conditions and
their inﬂuence on the fracture toughness utilizing the well known path independent J-
integral for both elastic and elastic-plastic material response. An analysis predicting the
crack propagation under critical loading will also be investigated and compared to the
experimental ﬁndings from a previous project by Juul [4] when possible. In this project the
analysis will be based on a numerical program being developed in Fortran by the author.
The project is divided into four main bullets, also giving the order in which the program is
being developed. The analyses being addressed during this project:
• Investigate critical conditions for the crack initiation in layered material systems.
Develop a numerical model capable of predicting the elastic response of a functionally
graded material (FGM), as-well as evaluate the stress intensity/energy release rate
by the J-integral.
• Analyze the eﬀect of an internal pressure on the crack surfaces originating from the
pressurized oil, by developing a numerical model to evaluate the eﬀect on the stress
intensity/energy release rate. This model should be including both elastic and elastic-
plastic material responses in a FGM.
• Understand the crack phenomenon taking place when a crack propagates in the ﬁlm
- either towards the substrate (edge crack) or along the substrate (interface crack).
Investigate the eﬀect of tilting the crack rather than assuming the crack to be perpen-
dicular to the substrate. Investigate the importance of the diﬀerent loading conditions
(normal pressure on the bearing surface, the crack face tractions for the internal oil
pressure and surface shear). Compare ﬁndings with experimental observation if pos-
sible.
• Predict crack propagation in the ﬁlm under critical loading conditions by developing
a numerical model utilizing the interaction integral procedure to determine the stress
intensity factors in a FGM. The stress intensity factors should be used to determine
the angle of propagation.
During the parameter study conducted in this project, all dynamic eﬀects will be ignored
for the sake of simplicity, even though it is a dynamic system which is analysed (purely
static study). In addition, all modelling will be performed in 2D under the assumption of
plane strain.
7
==PAGE:20==
==PAGE:21==
3 Numerical Modelling
The analysis of fracture toughness and crack propagation is based on an in-house developed
numerical model. The analysis of energy release rates for diﬀerent load scenarios will be
based on the path independent J-integral by Rice [5]. For the crack propagation study, the
interaction integral presented by Dodds et al. [7] will be utilized, which is based on the J-
integral approach. Both integrals are path independent and diﬃcult to evaluate analytically
for more complex problems, leading to the use of numerical evaluation based on the ﬁnite
element approach. In this project the numerical approach suggested by Shih et al. [6] is
utilized for evaluating the path independent integrals as domain integrals rather than line
integrals.
This chapter starts by giving a brief introduction to basic fracture mechanics which is
not a part of the numerical modelling, but is required in order to establish and explain
important concepts used in the project. The ﬁrst step is to establish the elastic and elastic-
plastic ﬁnite element program (also including a contact model) capable of determine the
displacements, stresses and strains, which are required for evaluation of the J-integral and
interaction integral (domain integrals). Next the numerical procedures for the J-integral
and interaction integral is presented which consist of post-processing the ﬁeld quantities.
The last part involving the domain integrals is interesting, as the approach for handling
layered materials, but also forces acting on the crack faces is not frequently encountered,
since only limited problems require this.
Essential numerical routines can be found in
Appendix F.
3.1
Basic Fracture Mechanics
In this part some basic deﬁnitions of fracture mechanics are summarized which form the
grounding for the crack analysis.
In fracture mechanics, crack initiation and growth can be described by three basic
modes. Figure 3.1 presents the three crack modes. The ﬁrst mode of fracture, Mode-I, is
the simplest and most studied mode of fracture which is know as the opening mode. In a
Mode-I crack, the load is applied normal to the plane and the displacement describe an in-
plane opening of the crack. The second mode of fracture, Mode-II, is known as the in-plane
shear mode (or sliding mode). Under a Mode-II condition, the loading is applied in-plane
and parallel to the crack surface. The displacement describe a sliding of the crack surfaces
relative to each other. In the third mode of fracture, Mode-III, known as out-of-plane shear
(or tearing mode), the loading is applied out of the plane and parallel to the crack surfaces.
The displacement in a Mode-III crack describe a tearing of the surfaces relative to each
other. In the numerical implementation in the 2D program, Mode-I or Mode-II (or a mix)
is considered (the Mode-III tearing mode is not considered further).
The general stress ﬁeld surrounding an elastic crack tip under generalized in-plane load-
ing, is an inﬁnite power series, where the leading term approaches an inﬁnite magnitude as
the evaluation point reaches the crack tip, while the remaining higher order terms is ﬁnite
9

(higher order terms are normally neglected). The stress ﬁeld in linear elastic fracture me-
chanics (LEFM) is governed by a 1/√r singularity at the crack tip, where r is the distance
from the crack tip to the point of evaluation as illustrated in Figure 3.2. The displacement
ﬁeld at the crack tip is proportional to √r. The stress and displacement ﬁelds which will
later be required for the interaction integral in Section 3.4 can be found in Appendix B.
In order to scale the stress singularity related to the Mode-I and Mode-II cracks, a
parameter called the stress intensity factor is used, where KI and KII is related to Mode-I
and Mode-II, respectively. The normal assumption for linear elastic fracture mechanics is
that fracture occurs when the stress intensity factors reaches a critical value related to the
material, i.e. K = Kc.
Elasticity and Near Crack Tip Plasticity
As mentioned, the elastic stress analysis predict an inﬁnite magnitude of the stresses at the
crack tip. However the stresses in a real material has to be ﬁnite as the crack tip radius
must be ﬁnite (Anderson [8]). The stresses around the crack tip will also be reduced due
to plasticity. For any material, fracture occurs in a plastic zone at the crack tip.
As it is assumed that the stress intensity factors uniquely describe the condition around
the crack tip, the plastic zone must be accounted for in the zone where the singularity
is dominant for the stress intensity factor to be a valid measure of fracture toughness.
The singularity 1/√r, does not apply to the plastic zone (for an elastic-plastic case the
Hutchinson, Rice and Rosengren (HRR) ﬁelds can be applied stating a 1/r singularity for
LEFM), however an estimate can be obtained under small scale yielding, given as:

As the material behaves plastically around the crack tip, the material will carry less
stress than it would if the material behaved elastically. This softening eﬀect in the plastic
zone can be accounted for according to Anderson [8], by deﬁning an eﬀective crack length

where a is the crack length.
The eﬀective stress intensity factor, Keff, can be obtained by inserting Eq. (3.2), into
an expression for K. Utilizing this, linear elastic fracture mechanics will be valid for fracture
under both elastic and small scale plastic conditions of material around the crack tip. The
criteria for failure will then be when Keff = Kc. The condition of small scale yielding
(SSY) is said to exist when the dimensions of a geometry is greater than 2.5

Path Independent J-Integral
Similar to the stress intensity factors which characterise a crack tip uniquely under a set
of assumptions, another fracture criteria exists for Mode-I, Mode-II and Mode-III (or a
mix) cracks. This is the energy release rate which is a measure of the energy available for
the crack to grow an increment. The relation between the stress intensity factors and the
energy release rate for a linear elastic problem, G, is given as:
G = 1

An extension was added to the energy release rate, enabling the energy release rate to
be used beyond linear elastics by Rice [5]. This is commonly known as the J-integral, which
is a path independent line integral performed around the crack tip as illustrated in Figure
3.4. The J-integral is expressed in Eq. (3.5).
J =
Z
Γ
(Wδ1i −σijuj,1)nidΓ
(3.5)
where W is the strain energy density given as W =
R εkl
0
σijdεij and uj, εij and σij is the
displacement, strains and stresses, respectively. The tractions are deﬁned as σijni, where
ni is the outward normal.
The J-integral is capable of describing the energy release rate in both linear elastic and
non-linear elastic problems. For the linear elastic case, J = G. The J-integral is valid for
non-linear problems as long as the problem apply to a number of assumptions which will
be discussed further during the derivation of the J-integral for the numeric implementation
(Section 3.3).
From Eq. (3.3) it is seen that the linear elastic J-integral (J = G for linear elasticity) is
related to a combination of the stress intensity factors. It is possible to decouple the relation
by a division of displacement and stresses into symmetric and anti-symmetric components,
so that KI and KII can be determined separately by the J-integral. This decomposition is
by some referred to as the interaction integral which is presented by for example Dodds et
al. [7]. The interaction integral is utilized in this project to separate the stress intensity
factors which can then be used to predict the crack propagation path. The procedure is
presented in Section 3.4.

3.2. THE FINITE ELEMENT METHOD
13
3.2
The Finite Element Method
The ﬁnite element method (FEM) is a widely used tool, which is solving ﬁeld problems
by a numerical approach based on the discretisation of a domain into a ﬁnite number of
elements. The ﬁnite element solution has the ability to converge against the exact solution
to a problem (for most problems, when an appropriate element formulation is chosen), as
the number of elements is increasing. The goal of the ﬁnite element method for this project
is to handle purely mechanical problems, where the ﬁeld variables are the displacements in
a 2D domain.
The numerical formulation of the domain integrals described in Section 3.3 and 3.4 is
utilizing the values which is the result of the ﬁnite element program. The program describe
the elastic (and later on, also the plastic) behaviour of the structure, and feed these results
into the domain integrals determining the energy release rate/stress intensity factor used
for analysing the fracture phenomena.
The ﬁnite element formulation can be derived based on the principle of virtual work.
The virtual work principle has the ability to establish a relation between variations in
internal and external work based on inﬁnitesimal displacement variations from which the
residual equations can be derived. In absence of body forces, the volume force equilibrium
can be expressed as:
σji,j = 0 in Ω
(3.6)
where Ωis the domain and σij is the stress component (symmetry applies).
Equilibrium of surface tractions acting on the surface of the domain, can be described
as:
σijni = Tj on ΓT
(3.7)
where Tj is the surface traction and ni is the the outward unit normal vector on the surface
ΓT at which the tractions are applied. In addition to the surface tractions, displacements
can also be prescribed. The prescribed displacements are expressed as:
ui = u∗
i
on Γu
(3.8)
where ui is the prescribed displacements in the points u∗
i on the surface Γu.
The equations presented in Eq. (3.6), (3.7) and (3.8) are also known as the strong form
of the governing partial diﬀerential equations and boundary conditions. The principle of
virtual work is based on the strong form of the partial diﬀerential equations. The diﬀerential
equations requires the ﬁeld of solution to be twice diﬀerentiable in the weak form, enabling
solutions to be obtained for a wide variety of geometries. The principle of virtual work can
be described as shown in Eq. (3.9) in absence of body forces.
Z

{δε}⊤[C]{ε}dΩ=

(3.9)
where vectors are denoted by {}, matrices are denoted by [], ε is the strains, u is the

stresses. In this project, the Cauchy relation is utilized stating εij = 1

transformed into auxiliary coordinates ξ and η, reaching from -1 to 1 (Cook et al. [11]).
The principle of virtual work can be interpreted in the way that the left hand side (Eq.
(3.9)) being the variation of the internal work (strain energy) and the right hand side being
the variation in the potential work of any external forces. This means that arbitrary virtual
displacement variations lead to identical variations in the internal and external work. This
will hold for both linear and non-linear system as well as conservative and non-conservative
systems.
The ﬁnite element program is based on the isoparametric formulation of an eight node
2D element (commonly known as the Q8 element, Cook et al. [11]). These elements are used
for the discretization of a domain, by subdivision into a mesh. Each element which represent
a small part of the structure (mesh), has its eight nodes, which is shared with neighbouring
elements. The nodes has both a local and a global number. The nodes represents the points
at which the ﬁelds variables are determined. The variables are also commonly referred to
as degrees of freedom (DOF). For the Q8 element used in this project, the ﬁeld variable of
interest is the displacements in x1- and x2-direction. This corresponds to two DOF in each
node i.e. the number of equations in the system will be two times the number of nodes in
the model. The Q8 element is presented in Figure 3.5, with the local position of the eight
nodes. The Q8 element is capable of having any arbitrary shape, the nodes can be collapsed
and the side lines can be curved (due to the isoparametric formulation). The isoparametric
formulation maps the arbitrary geometry of an element in physical coordinates into a set
of auxiliary coordinates where the shape in transformed into a square (see Figure 3.5).
When the elements are transformed into auxiliary coordinates, numerical integration can
be performed relatively easy. The numerical integration is based on Gaussian quadrature
and the number of Gauss points used is 2 by 2 corresponding to a reduced integration for
the Q8 element. In order to interpolate both displacement ﬁelds (or other ﬁeld quantities)
and the element geometry, a set of shape functions is utilized. The shape function used to
described the Q8 element is shown in Eq. (3.10), where ξ and η are auxiliary coordinates
(transformed coordinates) reaching from -1 to 1.

Numerical Procedure
As mentioned, the numerical integration scheme is based on Gaussian quadrature. The
Gaussian quadrature is summing the interpolated values in speciﬁc coordinates (related to
auxiliary ξ η space) of a domain utilizing the weight factors wi and wj in order to determine
the integral. An example of this procedure is shown for the stiﬀness matrix in Eq. (3.12),
determining the local contribution of each element which is afterwards gathered to the
global matrix.

and t is the element thickness (which is unity in this project)
3.2.1
J2 Flow Theory
As the behaviour is no longer linear elastic, a non-linear approach has to be implemented in
the model. The non-linearity will be dealt with by utilizing the Euler method. The plastic
behaviour of the material is described by the most commonly used J2 ﬂow theory. The J2
ﬂow theory is an elastic-plastic formulation, describing the elastic and plastic behaviour by
deﬁning a constitutive relation between strains and stresses. The J2 ﬂow theory is a rate
independent plasticity model.
In this project, it is assumed that small strain theory is valid for the models.
The
relation between displacements and strain is linear and described by εij = 1
2(ui,j + uj,i).
However the relationship between strains and stresses are non-linear. By these assumptions,
the strains and stresses will be described relative to the initial undeformed geometry. The
J2 ﬂow theory assumes that the material can be described by isotropic hardening, where
both the initial yield surface and subsequent yield surfaces are shaped as the von Mises
yield surface as illustrated in Figure 3.6.
The J2 ﬂow theory based on an incremental
approach which will also make is possible to model unloading. The theory which is used
for describing the plastic behaviour is following the approach of Sigmund et. al. [19].
Based on the von Mises theory, the plasticity is being associative, which means that
yielding takes place when the von Mises stress σe (Eq. (3.27)) reaches a limiting value. As
seen in Figure 3.6, non-linear behaviour arises when a the limit value is reached, which is
the yield stress σy. The yield stress is a material parameter related to the speciﬁc material.
When the yield stress is exceeded, the material behaves as an incompressible medium which
means that plastic strains are not associated with a volume change i.e. εp
kk (εp
ij is the plastic
contribution to the strain tensor).
σe =
1
√
2
h
(σ11 −σ22)2 + (σ22 −σ33)2 + (σ33 −σ11)2 + 6(τ 2
12 + τ 2
23 + τ 2
31)
i1/2
(3.27)
where in this case of plane strain τ23 and τ31 is zero.
If the yield surface is reached, then the yield surface will be expanded. This results in
that the largest obtained von Mises stress will be set as the new criterion for the stress
which has to be exceeded in order to perform further yielding. When the yield surface is
exceeded and the material starts to yield, Young’s modulus is no longer applying and a
tangent modulus Et, is required to describe the slope of the stress-strain curve. In order
to determine the tangent modulus, an expected behaviour has to be described. This could

modulus is a constant (typically a fraction of the Young’s modulus). For a more realistic
case (for metals), a power law could be applied in order to describe the behaviour in the
plastic region. In this project the power hardening law shown in Eq. (3.28) is utilized.

As the elastic part has already been established, the plastic part will be build on top of
this. First a yield function F, is established in order to have a criterion which can be used
to determine whether the material has started to yield. The yield function is shown in Eq.
(3.29).
F({σ}, ¯εp) = σe −σ0
(3.29)
where σ0 is the largest value of σe reached in previous plastic straining.
The case where F is negative will then describe elastic behaviour, and the case of F
equal to zero, will describe plastic behaviour. A case of F larger than zero, is not physically
possible, however numerically this is normally the case when plasticity occur. The reason is
that if F should be exactly zero, the steps that should be taken would have to be inﬁnitely
small. However for a large amount of steps in the incremental procedure, F ∼0.

3.2.2
Contact modelling
In order to account for the crack faces begin pressed against each other under certain loading
conditions, the elastic model has been further developed to account for contact. The contact
model is necessary as the crack faces overlap which is physically impossible, aﬀecting the
estimate of the energy release rate. The contact model is based on the principle of applying
a pressure on the parts of the surface which is in contact, corresponding to the pressure
surfaces in contact would be exerting on each other.
As the case of overlapping crack faces is not known before the displacement ﬁeld is
determined, the procedure is an iterative process. First the displacement ﬁeld solution is
determined, and if the crack faces are overlapping, a pressure is applied on the parts which
are in contact. The displacement used to determine whether the elements are overlapping
is based on the displacement perpendicular to the element face. This is found by taking the
dot product of the displacement vector and the unit normal vector (Figure 3.7) as shown
in Eq. (3.36).
U = {u} · {n}unit
(3.36)
where {u} is the displacement vector and {n}unit is the outward unit normal vector to the
element face.
The overlap or gap between element faces is then determined by adding the displace-
ments of each face, ∆= UB + UA. A negative ∆will indicate overlapping, according to
Figure 3.7 and a pressure will be added on the element. The principle of adding pressures
on element faces is illustrated in Figure 3.7, where the given procedure is performed on
each element on the crack surface. The displacement ﬁeld has to be reevaluated, and if
contact still occurs, the pressure is then further increased. This process will be repeated
until a pressure is applied on the surface such that the surfaces are coinciding, correspond
to being in contact. The model does not account for friction between the crack surfaces i.e.
any eﬀect arising from surfaces sliding, is not accounted for.
This model was developed for the elastic programs. For the plastic program, a contact
algorithm was never implemented, but the same algorithm could be utilized.
However
utilizing this algorithm for a plastic program would lead to a nested iteration loop causing
the computational time to increase signiﬁcantly. As the plastic approach never came to
x1

avoid elements from overlapping.

work, a more suitable contact model for the iterative plastic program was never investigated
which would have been needed to have a reasonable computational time. The numerical
procedure presented as pseudo code can be found in Appendix E.
3.3
The Domain J-Integral
As mentioned, the approach suggested by Shih et al. [6] has been utilized. Shih formulated
an energy domain integral methodology, which is the framework for the numerical analysis
of the J-integral. This technique can handle a large number of diﬀerent problems including
both quasistatic and dynamic problems with elastic, plastic or viscoplastic material be-
haviour. The method is able to handle ordinary loads such as forces and pressures, but also
thermal loadings. The numerical approach has proven to be very robust and eﬃcient, but
also lend itself nicely to implement in the ﬁnite element procedure, compared to previous
numerical approaches used for evaluating the J-integral. The excellent feature of the nu-
merical approach purposed by Shih, is that it is a domain integral instead of a line integral.
The domain integral ﬁts the numerical procedure as the evaluation follows the principles of
building the stiﬀness matrix and load vector.
The J-integral can be expressed on its general form as shown in Eq. (3.37), requiring
the contour surrounding the crack tip to be vanishing small.
J = lim

capable of taking various material behaviour into account through the strain energy density
W. The strain energy density can be expressed in term of the mechanical strain as shown
in Eq. (3.38).

As the numerical approach is based on the ﬁnite element procedure giving ﬁnite element
sizes, Eq. (3.37) is not suitable, since it is not possible to evaluate the stresses and strains
along a vanishingly small contour. In order to avoid this evaluation, the integral in changed
into a closed contour by connecting the inner and outer contours creating a surface (for
2D) as illustrated in Figure 3.8. By creating this contour integral, the inner contour Γ0
will be vanishingly small while the outer contour Γ1 will be ﬁnite. For the general case in
Eq. (3.37), only the inner contour Γ0 will yield the correct result as the contour has to be
vanishingly small, but for an elastically behaving quasistatic case, it is possible to evaluate
the J-integral at either Γ1 or Γ0. For a quasistatic case, the closed contour integral can be
written as Γ∗= Γ1 + Γ+ + Γ−−Γ0 (note the negative sign on Γ0 as mi and ni point in
opposite direction). The J-integral can now be determined as shown in Eq. (3.39), where
the ﬁrst term describe the contribution from the domain and the second integral describe
the contribution form the crack faces. In case of a traction free crack face, the second
integral will vanish.

required to be diﬀerentiable in x1-direction when investigating non-homogeneous materials.
This means that a direct transition from one material to another in x1-direction will yield
an error as the gradient goes to inﬁnity. However, a direct transition in x2-direction does
not yield an error as this simply leads to a zero gradient with respect to x1-direction.

The elastic part only exist for non-homogeneous materials, and the plastic part exist for
both non-homogeneous materials and for incremental plasticity. The reason for the need
of a correction when using incremental plasticity, can be explained by an example. By
having a conﬁguration characterized by incremental plasticity in a uniaxial stress state,
two neighbouring points, “a” and “b” which have the same value of uniaxial strain, ¯ϵ, can
be in a state where “a” has never experienced unloading and “b” is unloading (see Figure
3.9). The strain energy density at “a” and “b” (area under stress-strain curve), is diﬀerent
even though the state of strain is the same. If the plastic term in Eq. (3.50) was not
included, a zero change in strain energy density would be predicted going from “a” to “b”,
introducing an error.

this project has now been determined, the expression for the J-integral can established. By
inserting Eq. (3.47) into (3.40) and utilizing the expressions for the strain energy density,
an elastic-plastic, non-homogeneous expression for the J-integral is then obtained:

(Carpenter et al. [9]).

σ2j
∂uj
∂x1
qdΓ
(3.51)
The ﬁrst term of Eq. (3.51), describe the surface integral evaluating the J-integral for a
homogeneous material. The second term takes non-homogeneous materials and corrections
for incremental plasticity into account and the third term takes crack face tractions into
account. The commonly used J-integral only include the ﬁrst term, as non-homogeneous
material and surface tractions is normally excluded. The second term takes the material
gradients into account thought the strain gradients which arise from the changing material
parameters. This is needed in the analysis for functionally graded materials (FGM) which
is analysed later on. By investigating the second term it is also noted that this only have
an inﬂuence for material changes in x1 direction, i.e. cracks growing across material layers.
This means that this term is not necessary in case of an interface crack, where the crack
is growing along layers (between layers) instead of across layers. The third term including
crack face tractions is also normally excluded as tractions on the crack faces are normally
uncommon. The reason is that it is normally not possible to exert any force on a crack
face, as it is not possible to enter the crack. However in this special case of hydrodynamic
bearings, where the pressurized oil is capable of entering the crack, exerting a load on the
crack faces, it is necessary to include this term in order to take this eﬀect into account. In
addition this term is also required due to the contact model applying a pressure on the crack
faces. The eﬀect of a changing material and crack face tractions cannot just be omitted if
the eﬀects are present in the model, even though these eﬀect might not be of interest. The
reason for this is that the second and third term can be thought of as a correction term,
ensuring the path independence of the J-integral. This means that by just omitting these
terms, the integral would no longer be path independent, if any of these eﬀect exist and
thereby the concept of the J-integral no longer holds.
The q-function
The procedure transforming the J-integral into a domain integral required the mathematical
tool denoted as the q-function. As mentioned, the q-function is a function with the property
of being zero at the outer contour of the domain and one at the inner contour of the
domain, with a smooth behaviour in between. In order to get a better understanding of
the q-function, it can be thought of as a normalized virtual displacement. In the program
developed in this project, the q-function used, is a cone as illustrated in Figure 3.10. As
the theory describes, the shape can be arbitrary, but is required to be smooth. The cone
has nice features such as easy implementation and no corners which are not diﬀerentiable
except the top point where the value of the diﬀerentiated function is set to zero (unlike the
pyramid used by Shih, which has corners). The cone is restricted to having a value of 0 at
the outer contour and 1 at the inner contour. The expression describing the cone geometry
is shown in Eq. (3.52). The expression also allows the cone to be changed into a truncated
cone. In this program the geometry of a cone is solely used i.e. ri = 0.
q(re) =

where s is a shape factor. For s < 1 the cone will become concave and for s > 1 the cone
will become convex. The inﬂuence of the shape will be investigated later. As the program
is build in Cartesian coordinates, the distance to the point of evaluation can be described

needed is found by diﬀerentiating q with respect to x1 and x2.
Numerical Procedure
To implement the J-integral into the program, the expression in Eq. (3.51) should now be
changed into a ﬁnite element formulation. This is achieved by following the approach of
Dodds et al. [10]. As all numerical models in this project is in 2D, the area around the
crack tip has to be described as illustrated in Figure 3.8. The inner contour Γ0 is taken
to be at the crack tip, and thereby the surface integral corresponds to the area inside the
outer contour Γ1. As the J-integral is path independent the energy release rate can then be
evaluated at any distance from the crack tip. The evaluation of the outer contour Γ1 has
to coincide with the element boundaries according to Shih et al. [6], in order to obtain an
accurate result. This is diﬃcult when the area of evaluation is circular due to the choice of
the q-function (a cone) and in general a nuisance as the numerical model becomes diﬃcult
to describe in order for the area of evaluation to coincide with the element boundaries.
However the domain coinciding with the element boundaries has been found to be of less
importance as the computational power has increased signiﬁcantly, since the studies of
Shih, and a much ﬁner mesh can be utilized giving satisfactory results. The results has
negligible changes when altering the area of evaluation (domain size) depending on where
the elements are “cut" by the outer contour, but for a ﬁne mesh, the changes are below
0.1%.
The discretized form of the domain integral is shown in Eq. (3.53). The J-integral is
determined by summing up the contribution of the Gauss points p in each element, which is
within the outer contour Γ1. This procedure is similar to the procedure of determining the
element stiﬀness in ordinary isoparametric ﬁnite element programming. In addition, the
contribution of the crack face traction (if any) is summed up for element faces coinciding
with the crack faces. The procedure for determine the contribution from the crack face
tractions follows the same procedure as for applying a distributed force on the boundary in
a ordinary ﬁnite element model.

From Eq. (3.53) it is seen that the derivatives of the strain and strain energy density is
required. The derivatives of the strains and strain energy density, is determined numerically
as analytical functions are then not required. This is done by utilizing the values at the
Gauss points (integration points) and using an isoparametric extrapolation to determine
the nodal values followed by an interpolation to determine the derivatives at the Gauss
points (see Eq.
(3.54) and Eq.
(3.55)).
The method used for extrapolating the data
from the Gauss point to the nodes in each element follow an ordinary procedure utilizing
the shape functions of a 4-node element (Q4 element Cook et al. [11]), giving a linear
extrapolation. Normally during extrapolation, the values of coinciding local nodes (node
part of several elements), would be averaged, giving the value of the global node. During
the work with the J-integral in this project, it was found that due to the large gradients
around the crack tip (singularity), averaging would introduce an error, giving artiﬁcially
large gradients. In order to solve this problem, the values were not averaged, and the local
nodal values of each element was used for the interpolation. This was found to eliminate
the large gradients around the crack tip. Utilizing the local values without averaging has
no inﬂuence on the rest of the structure. The explanation is that, away from the crack tip,
the values are a smooth ﬁeld and therefore the diﬀerence between the local values of the
nodes is small and the averaging therefore does not make any signiﬁcant diﬀerences.
∂(εij)p
∂x1
=

X

∂NI

(3.55)
where the subscript p denote the values at the Gauss points, n is the number of element
nodes, subscript I is a node in an element and N is the shape function.
3.4
Interaction Integral
The interaction integral makes it possible to determine the stress intensity factors, KI
and KII, separately. Interaction integrals was developed from Betti’s reciprocal theorem
(Stern et al. [12]), the J-integral (Chen et al. [13]), and the L- and M-integrals (Knowles et
al. [14]). The work presented here, describe the interaction integral procedure to compute
stress intensity factors along cracks in 2D functionally graded materials under mechanical
loading in an elastic model. The procedure is based on the work presented by Dodds et al.
[7]. The procedure presented by Dodds, is related to three dimensional cracks, however in
the project the method is reduced to two dimensional plane strain assuming KIII = 0.
The interaction integral is build on the structure of the J-integral by Shih described
in Section 3.3. The interaction integral is also based on the results of the ﬁnite element
solution ( elastic solution). The interaction integral require a superposition of the actual
==PAGE:40==
28
CHAPTER 3. NUMERICAL MODELLING
computed equilibrium ﬁeld with the ﬁeld of an auxiliary equilibrium state chosen for the
purpose. The auxiliary ﬁeld serves as a benchmark to the situation expected around the
crack tip for Mode-I and Mode-II crack growth. By relating these two states of present state
(computed ﬁeld) and ﬁctive state (auxiliary ﬁeld), the individual contribution to Mode-I
and Mode-II crack growth can be determined (KI and KII). When the contribution to
the two modes is determined, it is afterwards possible to determine the direction (angle) in
which the crack will continue to grow.
As mentioned, the interaction integral is build on the work of Shih et al. [6], where the
integral is presented on a domain form utilizing the mathematical tool q in order to obtain
the energy release rate for a unit crack advance. By taking the expression for the J-integral
in Eq. (3.51) and superimpose the actual equilibrium ﬁelds and the auxiliary ﬁelds, the
superimposed domain integral, JS, is determined:
JS =

where superscript aux denote the auxiliary quantities.

and the strain energy from the interaction, as shown in Eq. (3.57).

using a numerical scheme. This will also save computational time as the values can be
found directly in the Gauss points without any need for storing the data. The derivatives
of the auxiliary ﬁeld quantities is found by utilizing the chain rule. The spatial derivatives
is denoted by (·),i = ∂(·)/∂xi and (·),ij = ∂(·)/∂xi∂xj. The ﬁrst order derivative of the ﬁeld

Interaction integral for FGM
As the auxiliary ﬁeld is based on a homogeneous material, it is necessary to compensate
for material gradients.
In order for the interaction integral to take functionally graded
materials into account, several methods exist. The method used in this project is based
on the work of Dolbow et al. [17], where the contribution from material gradients is taken
through the deﬁnition of auxiliary strain components as the product of auxiliary stresses and
the compliance matrix, Sijkl(x) accounting for varying material properties. The compliance
matrix can be found in Appendix B. The auxiliary strains can the be determined:
εaux
ij
= Sijkl(x)σaux
kl
(3.68)
The derivatives of the auxiliary strains is determined by applying the product rule to
Eq. (3.68):
εaux
ij,1 = Sijkl,1(x)σaux
kl
+ Sijkl(x)σaux
kl,1
(3.69)
The auxiliary stresses σaux
ij
, satisfy equilibrium, however the strain displacement com-
patibility is only satisﬁed with the material properties at the crack tip location s (for
functionally graded materials), as the auxiliary ﬁelds are based on the material properties
at this location. This results in that for x ̸= s:

as the stresses are know from boundary conditions and the displacements are determined
analytically. Thereby no numerical error is introduced in the third term accounting for
crack face tractions.

As an expression is now determined for the interaction integral, the energy release rate may
now be represented as a function of the mixed-mode stress intensity factors KI and KII.
The relation between the stress intensity factors and the energy release rate (for an elastic
material) is shown in Eq. (3.76) (Anderson [8]).
J = K2

When the stress intensity factors has been determined, it is possible to determine the
angle in which the crack will continue to grow from the current state. Several mixed mode
fracture criteria has been proposed to determine the crack growth direction such as the
maximum principle stress criterion or the strain energy density criterion. In this project it
is chosen to utilize the maximum stress criterion presented by Erdogan et al. [18].
Maximum Stress Criterion
The maximum stress criterion is based on the assumption that the crack will grow per-
pendicular to the maximum principal stress. Taking the stress ﬁelds around the crack tip
shown in Appendix B, and transforming these into a local polar coordinate system, the
following expressions is obtained:
σθθ =

stress.
The maximum principle stress direction is deﬁned by that the shear stress should be
zero:
σrθ = KI sin(θ) + KII(3 cos(θ) −1) = 0

In Eq. (3.84) it is seen that two solutions exist, where the solution giving the maximum
principle stress in then chosen. When the crack propagation angle can be determined at
a speciﬁc state, this method can be used for studying crack propagation. This is done by
incorporating a re-meshing scheme. An initial crack is modelled, and when the direction
which it will proceed in is determined, the model is re-meshed with an addition in crack
length in the direction previously found. This procedure is then continued, giving the crack
propagation path. The increment size of crack growth could be prescribed arbitrarily or be
determined by combining the model with for example Paris Law in order to determine the
length that the crack will propagate in each step.

4 Validation
In this chapter the previously developed numerical models will be validated against known
energy release rates/stress intensity factors for test specimen and will be compared with
the ﬁndings of scientiﬁc articles/commercial software utilizing similar numerical approaches.
During the validation of the diﬀerent models, it was chosen to only show a representative
number of the analysis which has been conducted.
4.1
Incremental Plasticity
After the numerical model has been expanded to also account for plastic behaviour accord-
ing to Section 3.2.1, the model is validated (also serves as validation for the elastic model).
The model has been validated, by comparing the results with the solution obtained by
the commercial package Ansys for an identical problem. Two diﬀerent problems used for
validation has been presented here. The ﬁrst test (shear test) is presented in Figure 4.1a.
The excellent feature about this test, is that the only component that exist in plane strain
is the shear component 12 (referring to tensor notation). By only having one component,
it is ideal for debugging and should coincide with Ansys, even though Ansys perform a
more complicated procedure involving both substeps and equilibrium equations in addition
to the load steps. The second problem used to validate the model, is a uni-axial tension
test. This test should have both a 11 component and a 33 component due to the plane
strain assumption. The comparison consists of making a stress-strain curve for the two
problem, with Ansys and the numerical program developed. If the numerical model work
as intended, the solutions should be equivalent.
In the shear problem in Figure 4.1a, a prescribed displacement is applied causing the
structure to yield and in the uni-axial tension test in Figure 4.2a a distributed load is
applied causing the structure to yield. The stress-strain plots are based on the reaction
force, F, divided by the area (thickness is always unity) and the strains are based on the
displacements divided by the original length.
Figure 4.1b presents the stress-strain curve for the shear test.
The stress has been
normalized by the shear yield stress τy =
1
√
3σy and the strain is normalized by the shear
yield strain γy = τy
G , where G is the shear modulus. When comparing the results of the
program and Ansys, it is seen that the results are coinciding both in the elastic and plastic
region as expected. It is observed that the curve enter the plastic stage at (1, 1), as it should
when using this normalization.
Figure 4.2b presents the stress-strain curve for the uni-axial tension test. Again the
results has been normalized by the expected yield stress and the expected yield strain.
Again, by observing the result, it is seen that the code and Ansys solution is coinciding as
expected. The yielding point is also where it was expected to be located ((1, 1) with this
normalization). These test give a good indication of the the numerical procedure in Section
3.2.1 has been implemented successfully.

large gradients occurring when q is evaluated close to the crack tip. As it can be seen
from the graph this becomes worse for smaller s (concave shape create large gradient at
the crack tip). In this project the shape factor is chosen to s = 1 a giving linear relation.
From the plot it is seen that this value of s gives a value very close to the analytical, even
for relatively large elements (fast converging solution).
4.3
Functionally Graded Materials
As the bearing which is the subject of this project consists of a layered material, the model
is compared to a scientiﬁc paper by Paulino et al. [22], using a similar approach to evaluate
the energy release rate of a functionally graded material. In this case the material is graded
in x1-direction meaning that the crack is growing perpendicular to the interface. For this
model, the second term in Eq. (3.51) will have a signiﬁcant inﬂuence taking the material
gradients in the material into account, ensuring the J-integral remains path independent.
The third term in Eq. (3.51), will remain zero as no crack face tractions are present. In
Figure 4.5a, the specimen is illustrated and in Figure 4.5b the material graduation over the
specimen is presented for varies factors of γa.
The graduation of the material follow the hyperbolic tangent function shown in Eq.
(4.1).
E(x1) = Es + Ef
2
+ Es −Ef
2
tanh(γ(x1 −xc))
(4.1)
where the subscript s denote the substrate (corresponding to the bearing backing material),
subscript f is the ﬁlm (corresponding to the bearing lining material), γ is a transition factor
and xc is the location of the mean value of the function (xc is zero in this case). The
transition zone is indicated in Figure 4.5a by the dashed line.
The load applied to the structure in Figure 4.5a, is scaled by the Young’s modulus i.e.
by the hyperbolic tangent function. The reason for using this type of distributed load it
that it ensures a uniform strain ﬁeld corresponding to a uniaxial tensile test. By utilizing
this, it is possible to establish the relationship presented in Figure 4.9 which will be used for
validating the crack face traction term in the J-integral. This would not have been possible
for a constant pressure with a non-homogeneous material as the strain ﬁeld would not be
uniform i.e. not corresponding to a uniaxial tensile test.
In this analysis, the values of Young’s modulus for the ﬁlm Ef = 3 and for the substrate
Es = 1 and Poisson’s ratio is 0.3 for both materials (Paulino et al. [22]). As seen in Figure
4.5b, the transition factor γ, changes the transition from at very smooth to an almost instant
change in material parameters. By investigating both negative and positive γ values, the
option of having both a stiﬀand soft ﬁlm relative to the substrate is investigated.
In
the bearings investigated later on in this project, the ﬁlm will always represent the softer
material.
Table 4.1 presents the results obtained by the program which is compared to the results
obtained by Paulino et al. [22]. From the analysis it is seen that the greatest diﬀerence is
3.41%. The results obtained by Paulino is also a result of a numerical calculation meaning
that an exact match would be unlikely due to numerical errors and the state of convergence.
Especially the diﬀerence of 3.41% is a result of a poor resolution of the material properties
in the transition zone, due to a very coarse mesh. Comparing this (and the other values)

4.3.1
Convergence
A convergence study has been performed on the structure with a functionally graded ma-
terial as presented in Figure 4.5a. Convergence is more diﬃcult to obtain for a functionally
==PAGE:52==
40
CHAPTER 4. VALIDATION
graded material. It was seen in Section 4.2, that convergence was obtained with the J-
integral for even few elements in the crack zone. For at functionally graded material this
was also expected, however the analysis revealed a diﬀerent problem when analysing a
graded material. Figure 4.6 presents a convergence study for the functionally graded struc-
ture in Figure 4.5a with diﬀerent transition zones. In Figure 4.6, it is observed that for
γa = 0 (homogeneous material), the solutions is already converged as rather ﬁne meshes are
used. As the value of γa increases, the solutions is no longer at a converged stage (γa = 6
and γa = 20). The reason for this is due to the resolution of the material properties in
the transitions zone. As the transitions zone becomes more narrow (but still has an exten-
sion), the number of elements describing the transition is decreasing. This is presented in
Figure 4.7 where the transitions zone is shown for both γa = 2 and γa = 20. It is seen
that the larger number of elements describing the transition zone for γa = 2, creates a
better resolution, also making the changes of the properties in the transition zone smaller
when the number of elements is changed. For γa = 20, the material properties will be
poorly deﬁned and when the element size is then reduced a signiﬁcant change in material
properties appears in the transitions zone. This is altering the structure, meaning that the
solution is altered, leading to a diﬀerent value of the J-integral. As the solution is already
converged for the homogeneous material this suggest that the lack of convergence seen in
the graph is actually a result of the continuously changing material properties due to the
increase in elements. When observing the value for γa = ∞in Figure 4.6, it is seen that
this value is also in a state of convergence. The reason for this is that the transitions is
sharp, giving no change in the transition zone properties when the number of elements is
changed, conﬁrming that the changing structure is the reason for the lack of convergence
in the cases with a narrow transitions zone. It should be noted that in the case of γa = ∞,
the material gradient is inﬁnite in the transition which will introducing an error in the
result. The reason is that the material variation in x1-direction should be diﬀerentiable as
described in the theory for the numerical models.

4.3.2
Crack Face Tractions
The model used for veriﬁcation has not yet included crack face traction. The validation
of this part plays an important role as later analysis will include a load on the crack faces
originating from the oil in the bearing and the contact model. Compared to the previous
analysis where the third term in Eq. (3.51) would remain zero, this term will now have
a contribution, ensuring the path independence. For this analysis all three term in Eq.
(3.51) is therefore necessary in order to obtain the correct value of the J-integral. In order
to validate the crack face traction term of the numerical model, the same model as in
the previous section has been analysed, but with equivalent crack face tractions, making
the results comparable.

superposition shown in Figure 4.9.

4.3.3
Interface Crack
In the previous section, an analysis was conducted in a specimen with the crack perpen-
dicular to the material interface (material graduation in x1-direction). In this section an
interface crack will be investigated i.e. the crack growing parallel to the material interface
(variation in x2-direction). As the crack is now growing along the material interface, the
material parameters will be constant in this direction. According to Eq. (3.51), the second
term of the J-integral will therefore vanish as there is no material variation in this direction.
The model does not involve crack face tractions either i.e the third term of Eq. (3.51) will
also vanish. Due to this, cracks of this type can be analysed considering only the ﬁrst term
of Eq. (3.51). The analysis is based on a far ﬁeld KI, as for the analysis in Section 4.2
investigating the q-function. As the material is not homogeneous a diﬀerent displacement
ﬁeld is required in order to obtain a pure Mode-I crack as illustrated in Figure 4.3. The
==PAGE:55==

conducted both including and excluding the second term of Eq. (3.51). In Table 4.3 it
is seen that the results coincide conﬁrming that the second term is zero. When observing
the values it is seen that the values coincide for α < 0, which correspond to having a soft
ﬁlm compared to the substrate. When α > 0, the results are gradually deviating from the
the analytical result, overestimating the energy release rate. The reason for the poor ﬁt of
the results with α > 0, is that a singularity is approached. From the article of Beuth [26],
investigating a similar problem it is seen that values start to increase with an exponentially
rate at α > 0.5, which is also where this numerical procedure begins to deviate signiﬁcantly.
However as this project is based on a problem where the ﬁlm is the softer material, this
issue will not have any inﬂuence on the results obtained.
4.4
Elastic-Plastic J-Integral
Validating the plastic contribution to the J-integral has proven to be more diﬃcult than
expected, as only few papers based on a similar numerical approach has been published. Few

papers from R.H. Dodds and W. C. Carpenter has been published, however these papers
only present results in 3D or the numerical model includes cohesive elements making result
incomparable. The reason for this is believed to be due to numerical error which remain
unsolved for this approach to the plastic J-integral.
In order to validate the plastic contribution to the J-integral, the result is compared
to Ansys solutions, as Ansys has developed a feature which can evaluate the J-integral for
elastic-plastic materials. However Ansys makes a very common assumption of that the
contribution of the second integral in Eq. (3.51) vanishes for proportional loading, even
though incremental plasticity is utilized.
The approach has been divided into two parts. First the path independence is investi-
gated and secondly the magnitude of the results is compared against solutions obtained in
Ansys. When comparing to Ansys, the results is compared both when ignoring the second
integral of Eq. (3.51) and with the second integral, which should serve as a correction for
lack of proportionality in the incremental theory.
The ﬁrst test is based on the domain and displacement ﬁeld utilized in Section 4.2
which can be found in Appendix B. The magnitude of the plastic zone is presented for two
diﬀerent KI in Figure 4.10. In Figure 4.10, diﬀerent magnitudes of KI is a applied, giving
a small and a large plastic zone relative to the size of the model, in order to check the path
independence. The J-integral is evaluated at a large distance from the crack tip, making
the contribution from the plasticity small compared to the total value, and afterwards the
J-integral is evaluated close to the crack tip, making the contribution from the plastic zone
large compared to the total value. These values should be coinciding, conﬁrming the path
independence is maintained and prove that the plastic contribution from the second integral
in Eq. (3.51) corrects for the incremental plasticity. Table 4.4 presents the values of the
J-integral (for model with small plastic zone) for diﬀerent values and magnitudes of the
outer contour Γ1 according to Figure 3.8. It is seen that there are minor ﬂuctuations in
the values due to how the contour is positioned relative to the elements, but overall the
value is constant when the second term of Eq. (3.51) is included. The values are identical
for a large contour of radius 5 according to Figure 4.10a, where the plastic zone is small
compared to the total area and a small contour of radius 0.3, where the plastic zone is a
signiﬁcant part of the area which is evaluated. This indicates that the plastic contribution
coming from the second term in Eq. (3.51), is working as intended. When comparing to
the values in Table 4.4 where the second term of Eq. (3.51) is omitted, it seen that the

for maintaining path independence. Due to this the path independence has become worse,

From this analysis, is was found that the correction term ensures the path independence
as expected, but it also contributes to a signiﬁcant diﬀerence in actual value compared to the
results without the correction. In order to investigate the diﬀerence in magnitude further,
studies is performed comparing to Ansys results.
The secondly test investigate the magnitude of the results, as a signiﬁcant diﬀerence
was observed in the previous results when comparing the calculations with and without
the correction. The analysis compare results obtained Ansys and the present program for
the edge cracked specimen illustrated in Figure 4.11. As mentioned, Ansys do not include
the second term of Eq. (3.51), under the assumption of that incremental plasticity without
unloading should be close to proportional and therefore the second term should be close
to zero. The analysis was performed with a constant tangent modulus Et = 2450, Young’s
modulus E = 205000, Poisson’s ratio ν = 0.3 and a prescribed displacement ∆= 2.
The results of the analysis is presented in Table 4.6, comparing the results obtained
by Ansys to the results obtained by the code, with and without the correction (second
term of Eq. (3.51)). The studies are performed for a varying yield stress, to investigate
the inﬂuence of the plastic zone size on the diﬀerence in results. When comparing the
results obtained by Ansys to the results from the code without the correction, it is seen
that the results are in compliance, suggesting that the implementation of the plastic part

and numerical evaluation function as intended. When comparing to the results where the
correction is included, it is seen that for large amounts of plasticity (see Figure 4.12) and
no plasticity (elastic problem), the results are satisfactory. For the state in between, where
a relatively small plastic zone exist around the crack tip, the results starts to deviate
signiﬁcantly indicating a problem with the correction term. It was expected that for a
high yield stress where the solution is purely elastic, that the results would match as this
has already been veriﬁed. However for low yields stress with large amounts of plasticity,
the solution also match which was unexpected since the solutions in between deviate. A
possible explanation for this is that the stresses near the crack tip at some point reach a
saturated value in incremental plasticity leaving only the strain derivatives to be singular
at the crack tip as suggested by Dodds [27]. The same tendency as for the previous study
is still seen where the results with the correction, is giving a lower estimate of the value.
After further investigation into this problem, it was found in the literature that both
Carpenter et al. [9] and Dodds [27] had encountered the same problem, when including
the second term of Eq. (3.51), in the domain based fashion presented by Shih. In the
literature no actual solution was found to this problem. In order to avoid the problem,
the suggestions involved ignoring the second term of Eq. (3.51), under the statement of
incremental plasticity with monotonic loading being almost proportional (same approach
as Ansys chose). Another approach is not to evaluate the second term close to the crack
tip as this is where the problem arise, however this is eﬀectively the same as ignoring the
second term. Another approach could be to implement a deformation theory rather than an
incremental theory, however neither of these solutions is an option as this project deals with
==PAGE:60==

CHAPTER 4. VALIDATION

Figure 4.12: Plastic zone around the crack tip in edge-cracked specimen under tension for diﬀerent
magnitudes of yield stress.
functionally graded materials which makes the second term necessary due to the material
gradients.
As no solution to the problem was found in the literature, the problem was investigated
further in an attempt to ﬁnd a solution. In order to ensure that the second term of Eq. (3.51)
should only have a very small contribution due to the lack of the load being proportional,
an additional program based on deformation theory was also developed, according to the
approach presented by Tvergaard [20] (it should be noted that thorough validation of this
code was never conducted). The results obtained by implementing a deformation theory
is also presented in Table 4.6.
It is seen that for high yields stresses σy > 2000, the
results coincide, due a small plastic region and the lack of proportionality being small.
For a low yield stress where the plastic zone is very large, there is a minor diﬀerence in
the results, which was expected as the correction term becomes more important when the
plastic zone size is increasing. This conﬁrms that it is valid to omit the correction term
as it only has a negligible contribution for incremental theory with monotonic loading (for
large plastic zones it is not entirely valid). However this is still only a valid approach for
homogeneous materials as no material gradients exist. During the analysis conducted with
the deformation theory it was also found that the second term in Eq. (3.51) still introduces
the same error if included, indicating that the problem is not induced by the incremental
plasticity formulation.
As an incremental theory was utilized in this project, the proportionality of the edge-
cracked specimen was investigated to get an idea of the importance for a correction. This
was done by utilizing Eq. (4.8), which compare the direction of the total stress vector with
the stress increment. For proportional loading these vectors should be parallel, giving a
value of 1. Figure 4.13 presents the results of the proportionality study. It is seen that for
==PAGE:61==

solution where plasticity exists, it is seen that areas which are not proportional exist in the
regions where the plastic zone is located (see Figure 4.12). However even though these large
regions where the proportionality condition is not fulﬁlled exist, the contribution should
still be small as was found in the previous studies.
