= 1
(4.8)
To better understand the problem, the contribution from the second integral was in-
vestigated further. These investigations showed that, when dividing the second term in an
elastic and plastic part as in Eq. (3.50), the error is solely coming from the plastic part,
and the elastic part is ∼0 as was also shown in the elastic J-integral for a homogeneous
material (also holds for deformation theory). Further investigations also showed that the
contribution more speciﬁcally came from the elements surrounding the crack tip (approx.
three element radius), leading to a signiﬁcant error. The near crack tip elements has the
most signiﬁcant contribution as the plastic work and the plastic strains change rapidly in
x1-direction at this location. In Eq. (3.51), it is seen that the ﬁrst and second term include
the same variables, however in the second term it is the derivatives. As the J-integral, ig-
noring the second term, gives the desired result, it implies that the magnitudes are correct,
however the derivatives remain incorrect, yielding a wrong results. Due to this a suggestion
of why the ﬁrst term yield the correct value, but the second term including derivatives do
not was made. The suggestion is that the actual values might be wrong, but due to the
nature of the problem it has no inﬂuence. The reason for this is that an investigation of the
contributions showed that the values in the Gauss points before the crack tip is negative
and the values after the crack tip is positive. If the error is of the same magnitude, this will
not have any inﬂuence on the result of the ﬁrst term, since the error will cancel out, however
==PAGE:62==
50
CHAPTER 4. VALIDATION
it will have an inﬂuence on the second, including the derivatives. For example if a certain
value should be -10 before the crack tip and 20 after the crack tip, but an error makes the
actual value -15 and 25, so when the values are integrated (summed up numerically), the
result is the same, however the gradient will be diﬀerent.
As it is indicated that the derivatives are wrong, even though the approach used to ﬁnd
the derivatives is identical to the approach in the purely elastic study where the results are
correct, other schemes of determining the derivatives were investigated. The scheme used
for currently ﬁnding the derivatives, is based on linear extrapolation and the isoparametric
formulation, however this lead to a constant value of the derivatives within an element.
The constant value of the derivatives within an element was suggested by Carpenter et al.
[9] to be the source of the error, as the rapid changes close to the crack tip would not be
included.
The ﬁrst approach in using a diﬀerent scheme was based on the ﬁnite diﬀerence ap-
proach, determining the value of the derivative in a Gauss point based on the values in the
neighbouring points. This approach did not solve the problem, but gave identical results
to the original scheme for ﬁnding the derivatives.
The possibility of full Gauss integral was also discussed, and performed with both linear
and quadratic extrapolation. A quadratic extrapolation was tested by utilizing the shape
functions of a 9-node element (Q9, Cook et al. [11]). The results of this attempt is presented
in Table 4.6 under the column “Q9”. This improved the results in some cases, but the results
became worse in order cases giving no deﬁnitive improvement. Especially the elastic case
(σy = 50000), where the value has been veriﬁed gives a poor result indicating that this
attempt did not solve the problem (or atleast also introduce a new one). This indicate that
the problem is not solely due to constant values in the Gauss points as Carpenter et al. [9]
suggested. Full integration was also tested while keeping the linear extrapolation, but with
no improvements in the results. It was also found by Dodds [27] that full integration lead
to worse results, due to problems such as shear locking.
The next approach which was attempted was signiﬁcantly more advanced.
In this
approach a singularity was incorporated in the scheme by utilizing a basis function from
X-FEM, presented by Elguedj et al. [28]. The basis function is shown in Eq. (4.9), where
the characteristics of the singularity can be changed by changing n (θ and r are the polar
coordinates).
F(r, θ) = r
1
n+1 [c1 sin(θ/2) + c2 cos(θ/2) + c3 sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2)
+ c4 sin(θ) cos(θ/2) + c5 sin(θ/2) sin(3θ) + c6 cos(θ/2) sin(3θ)]
(4.9)
In order to use this function for ﬁnding the derivatives needed, a data ﬁtting algorithm
was developed and implemented in the code.
This algorithm was required in order to
determine the constants c1 to c6. Due to the nature of the equation, linear data ﬁtting
could be used based on the least square method. After the implementation in the code, the
function was utilized to determine the derivatives. This was done for diﬀerent values of n.
When performing the least square method to determine the constants, the amount of values
around the crack tip used in the estimate were varied. When the constants were determined
it was tried to only use the values based on the function in Eq. (4.9) for the elements around
the crack tip, but also for the entire domain. The results of this was that the best estimates
of the J-integral was obtained when determining the constant with the least square based
on a large number of values as it smoothed potential errors. It was also found that the best
estimate was obtained when n = 0, which correspond to having no singularity, which was
unexpected as singular behaviour around the crack tip is expected. At ﬁrst this procedure
seemed to be a possible solution to the problem. In order to further prove this, the method
==PAGE:63==
4.4. ELASTIC-PLASTIC J-INTEGRAL
51
(a) Regular elements around crack tip.
(b) Crack tip elements.
Figure 4.14: Diﬀerent mesh schemes used around the crack tip.
using a X-FEM basis function, was used in the purely elastic code with a functionally graded
material, as this approach should yield results identical to what was previously found, if
the approach works as intended. However the values for the functionally graded materials
using this approach gave too low estimates, suggesting that this approach did not solve the
problem. This suggests that the approach gave the correct results, due to the least square
method smoothed out any errors, but also the actual behaviour, rendering this approach
not a possible solution.
During the work with this problem it has also been tried to use diﬀerent mesh schemes
around the crack tip. This include ordinary regular meshes, but also crack-tip elements
as presented in Figure 4.14, which produce a strain singularity. When trying out diﬀerent
mesh schemes, it was found that the results are very sensitive to the mesh. Reﬁning mesh
or using crack-tip elements gave better results in some conﬁgurations, but worse results for
other conﬁgurations. However there was no uniquely relationship between the mesh and
the results suggesting that a more advanced mesh could solve the problem. This is also in
accordance with the ﬁndings of Dodds [27]. After several approaches had been tested out
in order to solve the problem without any luck, the same conclusion was reached as Dodds
[27] did. That it is due to numerical error in the estimation of the values around the crack
tip used for the second term of Eq. (3.51), the evaluation of non-linear problems does not
appear promising.
Several entirely diﬀerent approaches was discussed which could solve the problem.
Among these approaches was that the second term could be evaluated as a contour in-
tegral instead of the approach by Shih transforming it into a domain integral, since the
values around the crack-tip could then be excluded from the calculation (evaluating the
contour far from the crack tip). However this approach is a diﬀerent and more troublesome
approach which does not ﬁt as well into the ﬁnite element formulation, as Shih’s method
do. This would also make the program less ﬂexible as the contour should be clearly deﬁned
where the method by Shih is an evaluating of an area which is much more versatile. As a
very complicated approach to this problem is was also discussed whether implementation
of strain gradient plasticity could solve the problem, as the gradients of the values would
then be directly available. Strain gradient plasticity is however very diﬃcult to implement
in an isotropic code. Gradient plasticity would possible also lead to that the J-integral
would have to be further investigated as contributions from the gradient approach might
have to be added. These approaches were not considered to implement as the goal was a
fairly simple approach based on the domain integral rather than a contour integral, as this
is an elegant approach ﬁtting well into the ﬁnite element formulation. Therefore solution
based on the domain integral without implementing much more complicating schemes such

as gradient plasticity were the primary focus.
As the implementation of the plastic J-integral did not appear promising, the use might
seem small, however certain problems in which the second term is not required can still be
analysed. These problems are mainly interface cracks. When handling interface cracks, the
graduation of the material is in x2-direction and therefore the elastic part of Eq. (3.50)
is zero. Then by using the assumption of that incremental plasticity without unloading is
very close to proportional, the plastic part of Eq. (3.50) can be omitted (however this has
a slight eﬀect on the path independence). The plastic part could also be omitted by using
deformation theory instead of incremental theory.
Examples of other problems which could also be analysed, is models such as the ones
presented in Figure 4.15. Common for these models is that the crack-tip is in an elastic
region, and thereby the errors which exist around the crack-tip due to plasticity is avoided.
It should be noted that the transition from elastic to elastic-plastic has to be smooth, since
the values should be diﬀerentiable with respect to x1 direction. This smooth transition
could be achieved by using a material grading of the yield stress (similar to the grading
of other material parameters). However these models are of no use in this project as the
model in Figure 4.15a suggests that the crack grow into the much harder substrate which
is highly unlikely. The model in Figure 4.15b, suggest that the much harder substrate is
behaving elastic-plastic while the softer ﬁlm is only acting elastic, which is also unlikely for
the type of problem analysed in this project.
As the elastic-plastic code did not appear promising and validation of the results showed
a signiﬁcant error, it was chosen not to use the elastic-plastic model in the parameter study
as it would not be possible to draw any reliable conclusions from these studies. The interface
crack study could be performed, however it was chosen not to conduct this study either.
4.5

After the interaction integral has been implemented, based on the procedure presented in
Section 3.4, the program is validated. The validation include three models. The ﬁrst study
is performed utilizing the displacement ﬁeld of the auxiliary ﬁeld as a boundary condition

As for the study in Section 4.2, the displacement
ﬁeld is applied on the boundary of the structure.
The interaction integral should then
yield the same stress intensity factors as used for the displacement ﬁeld applied on the
boundary. Table 4.7 presents the results of the study. From the table, the values of the
stress intensity factors applied when prescribing the displacement on the boundary, and the
values calculated by the program is shown. Is is seen that the results are coinciding, and
only minor diﬀerences is observed. These diﬀerences are a result of numerically induced
errors when performing the calculations.
In addition to the study of the magnitude, a
study of the path independence is also conducted. This is conducted with a constant K-
ﬁeld applied of KI = 5 and KII = 2. The results are presented in Table 4.8, where it is seen
that a negligible variation exists, when changing the size of the evaluated domain. This
conﬁrms that integral is path independence as it should be.
The second model used for validating, involve functionally graded materials. This model
is identical to the model in Section 4.3, regarding both the structure, load and grading of
the material. In this part, the crack face traction term of the integral is also validated. In
Table 4.9 it is seen that for this analysis, the results are compared to the work of Eischen
[16], rather than Paulino et al. [22] as in Section 4.3. The reason for comparing with two
diﬀerent sources, is that Paulino uses a similar method for calculating the value of the J-
integral and Eischen uses a similar method of calculating the interaction integral. There is
a diﬀerence in the result depending on which model is used, even though the results should
be identical, but the diﬀerence is small. The diﬀerence in the methods is most likely a
result of the exact numerical procedure, but is also depending on the auxiliary ﬁeld chosen.
In case the problem is related to the choice of auxiliary ﬁeld, this could be avoided by
introducing a diﬀerent auxiliary ﬁeld, as this can be chosen arbitrarily. A procedure for
determining the auxiliary ﬁelds numerically, based on a given structure, was presented by
Kim et al. [29]. However the procedure is not easily conducted as it is necessary to create
pure Mode-I and Mode-II cracks in a given structure in order to determine the auxiliary
ﬁelds. In the literature, the most commonly used approach is to utilize the auxiliary ﬁelds
presented by Williams [15], which has also been done in this work, yielding satisfactory
results.
Examining the results obtained in Table 4.9, it is seen that there is a satisfying correla-
tion in the results. Comparing results from Eischen with the results obtained by applying
the force at the boundary, it is seen that the maximum diﬀerence is 0.59%, which is satis-
factory. It should also be noted that as discussed in the previous sections, the functionally
graded materials are very mesh sensitive, due to resolution of material parameters, and

based on a semi-inﬁnite plate, a very large (dimensionally) ﬁnite element model has to be

5 Parameter Study of Cracks in
Hydrodynamic Bearings
In this chapter, the numerical models presented in Chapter 3 will be used for conducting
the parameter study of cracks in hydrodynamic bearing. The chapter is divided into two
parts where the ﬁrst part of the study is based on the J-integral determining the energy
release rate and the second is based on the interaction integral used for determining the
crack propagation path. Originally an elastic-plastic part was only intended, but due to
the numerical diﬃculties, this part was omitted.
Figure 5.1 illustrate the diﬀerent types of cracks and loading situations investigated
in this study. In Figure 5.1 to 5.1c, the diﬀerent load types acting on the specimen are
illustrated. This includes a normal load on the surface originating from the load carried by
the bearing, an internal pressure on the crack faces due to the pressurized oil entering the
crack (function of normal pressure on surface) and a surface shear traction originating from
the viscous forces and metallic contact. In the parameter study, individual or combined
loading situations are applied to a model. In each parameter study, the loading scenario
will be given (the combined load case refer to all three load types applied at once). For
the models utilized in the study, the surface shear is always acting in the direction of the
coordinate axis parallel to the bearing surface. When conducting the parameter study, some
of the loading situations are artiﬁcial and others are a representation of what is expected
in a hydrodynamic bearing. Artiﬁcial loading scenarios are also investigated to determine
the eﬀect of the diﬀerent loads. The load scenarios investigated are listed below:
• Surface shear - Artiﬁcial case as a normal load will exist on the surface due to the
load on the bearing.
• Normal load - Realistic case for an ideally running bearing where oil is not able to
enter the crack.
• Crack face tractions - Artiﬁcial case since pressure acting on crack faces arise from
the normal load.
• Normal load and crack face traction - Realistic case for an ideally running bearing
where oil is able to enter crack.
• Normal load and surface shear - Realistic case where the oil is not able to enter crack.
• Crack face traction and surface shear - Artiﬁcial case since pressure acting on crack
faces arise from the normal load.
• Normal load, crack face traction and surface shear - Realistic case where the oil is
able to enter crack.

Figure 5.1: Various crack types and load cases in layered material structure.
The type of cracks, which the diﬀerent load cases (or a combination of load cases) can be
applied to is illustrated in Figure 5.1d to 5.1f. These models include an edge crack, which
is having the length a and is at an angle φ, an interface crack between the material layers
and an edge crack which has grown to the interface and then deﬂected, growing along the
interface. For all model the distance to the center of the material transitions zone (ﬁlm
thickness) is denoted d.
As this study is simpliﬁed to a 2D model, each model should be though of as a 2D
section corresponding to the lower hatched area shown in bottom of Figure 1.1a (for a
thrust bearing) or the lower section of the bearing shown in Figure 1.3b (same section of
the whole journal bearing shown in Figure 1.1b).
In this parameter study the units are as follows: lengths are in millimetre [mm], stress-
es/pressures are in mega pascal [MPa] and angles are in degrees [◦]. Due to this combina-
tion, the unit of the energy release rate will be energy (Joule) per unit fracture surface area
[mJ/mm2] and the unit of the stress intensity factor will be stress times the squared root
of the length [MPa · mm1/2].
5.1
Elastic Parameter Study
As a ﬁrst approach to the analysis of the fracture toughness, a parameter study based
on the elastic model is conducted. The analysis will consider the three loading conditions
illustration in Figure 5.1a to 5.1c and a combination of the loads. A study with varying
crack length a will also be performed for selected load cases. The analysis of the loads, is
conduced by moving the load over the crack opening in several static steps, to investigate
the inﬂuence as the load in moving across the crack opening.
This correspond to the
scenario presented in Figure 5.2. The normal load is approximated with the normalized
normal distribution shown in Eq. (5.1). As the normal distribution has been normalized,
the value at the center will always be unity. The normal distribution is then multiplied by
a maximum pressure P, giving this value at the center of the normal distribution and a
value going towards zero when moving away from the center. The magnitude of the surface

In this analysis the parameter µ describe the position of load (center of normal distribu-
tion) with respect to the crack opening on the surface and is varied in the static steps (see
example in Figure 5.3b). The moving direction of the load is always in the direction of the
coordinate axis parallel to the surface as for the surface shear direction. In all parameter
studies performed, the standard deviation for the normal distribution is kept constant at
σ = 0.5. The maximum pressure in the bearing is set to P = 40 which is used to scale
all the loads, in order to obtain the correct ratio between the loads. The magnitude of P
which is used for scaling the loads is based on the theoretical values obtained by Juul [4].
The internal pressure on the crack faces will be a function of the magnitude of the normal
pressure on top of the crack opening (when the normal load is not applied, the pressure in
the crack is as if the normal load was applied). Under the analysis, the material properties
of the white metal lining (ﬁlm) is Ef = 50000 and νf = 0.33. The material properties of the
steel backing (substrate) material is Es = 210000 and νs = 0.3. The analysis is conducted
on the model illustrated in Figure 5.3a, with an inﬁnite sharp edge-crack. The backside is
constrained corresponding to having a rigid bearing housing (in reality the housing is not
rigid). The normal distribution used for describing the load is illustrated in Figure 5.3b,
where the crack opening is located at x = 0. The parameter µ then describe the distance
from the crack opening on the surface, to the center of the normal distribution. As men-
tioned, the normal distribution is multiplied with P = 40 and for the surface traction T,
the normal distribution is multiplied by a factor shown in the plots. The material transi-
tion is modelled by the hyperbolic tangent function shown in Eq. (4.1), with a transition
factor γ = 10 for both Young’s modulus and Poission’s ratio. This will result in a narrow
transition zone similar to the graph for γa = 20 in Figure 4.5b. The narrow zone is a good
approximation for a bearing, as the steel backing is produced ﬁrst and then the white metal
is cast on the surface (centrifugal casting).
As a ﬁrst step in the analysis a convergence study is conducted. The study is conducted
based on the structure in Figure 5.3a with only the surface normal load acting, described
by the normal distribution (P = 40). The convergence study is performed for diﬀerent
material transitions, according to Eq. (4.1). The same behaviour is observed as described
in Section 4.3.1, where the solution for γ = 10 has not converged, but this is a result of the
changing material properties in the transition zone. For the following parameter study, the

comparable the same element size is used for all studies in this chapter giving the same
resolution of the transition zone.
For the initial parameter studies of the edge-crack, the deformed shape of the structure
was investigated. This revealed a problem, which the numerical model was not able to
handle at ﬁrst. The problem was that for speciﬁc loading conditions, the material was
overlapping as the elastic model did not account for potential contact. This is presented
in Figure 5.5, where the material is overlapping when the normal load is positioned on top
of the crack. Additional ﬁgures of other load cases are shown in Appendix A. In order to

5.1.1
Parameter Study of Edge-Crack
After the initial studies of convergence and an upgrade of the model to include contact, the
ﬁrst actual studies could be conducted. Figure 5.6 and 5.7 present the parameter studies
of diﬀerent load scenarios moving over the edge-crack. The plot reaches from µ = −2.2 to
µ = 2.2, which is necessary as the surface shear is not symmetric (if all loads were symmetric
only half the interval should be modelled). All the models consider contact between crack
faces and account for this behaviour. In Appendix A, the same model without contact
included in the numerical model can be seen, in order to see the eﬀect of the contact model.
Figure 5.6a presents a study only including the surface shear for diﬀerent magnitudes of
shear T (related to the maximum pressure P by a fraction). The tendency is the same for
the diﬀerent magnitudes of the load. It is observed that when the surface shear is moving
towards the crack (from negative to positive µ), the energy release rate is slowly increasing,
but the value is still relatively small compared to the maximum. This is due to the shear
acting in the positive x2 direction in Figure 5.3a, pressing the crack faces against each other
giving a closing eﬀect on the crack. The value is not going to zero under this closure of
the crack as a Mode-II behaviour, arises at the same time when the surface is sheared.
When the surface shear is located above the crack (µ = 0), the energy release rate starts
to increase as the crack will now be opened. The increase is steep and reaches a maximum
at around µ = 1.2. When the load is moving further away, the energy release rate start
to decrease again. From this it is seen that the most damaging situation regarding surface
shear is actually not when the load is just on top of the crack, but at a distance after
the crack, when the shear is giving its maximum contribution to opening the crack (in a
Mode-I).

Figure 5.6: Parameter study of perpendicular edge-crack with γ = 10 and P = 40.
Figure 5.6b presents a study of surface shear and normal load. The surface shear is
varied in order to determine the contribution to the energy release rate compared to the
normal load.
In this problem where the normal load is pressing down the surface, the
contact model becomes very important as overlapping will occur for almost all values of µ.
For T/P = 0 i.e. no shear, it is seen that the graph is symmetric as the whole problem
becomes symmetric. For T/P = 1 where the shear and normal load are of equal size, it
==PAGE:75==
5.1. ELASTIC PARAMETER STUDY
63
is seen that the maximum energy release rate is found when the load has not passed the
crack opening. This was unexpected as the shear in an isolated case had its maximum
contribution when the load was positioned after the crack and the isolated normal load
is giving a symmetric contribution. As both the shear and normal load would contribute
to closing the crack when acting at this position, the large energy release rate is most
likely to originate from a Mode-II crack behaviour.
This indicate that the principle of
superposition cannot just be applied even though the solution is based on an elastic case
since a combinations of load scenarios can create an entirely diﬀerent eﬀect (in additions,
the contact model also introduce non-linearity). For the case of surface shear and normal
load, the maximum contribution is found at approximately µ = −0.8. For large amounts of
surface shear (T/P = 1), the position of the minimum energy release rate is when the load
is acting after the crack. After the minimum is reached, the energy release rate is increasing
again and has a local maximum much later than the other cases for a lower surface shear.
When the magnitude of the surface shear is decreased, the position of the maximum energy
release rate remains the same, but the minimum is moving against µ = 0. When the shear
is decreasing, the loading situation is going toward a symmetric state as the normal load
becomes dominant. At this state is seen that for µ = 0, the energy release rate is close to
zero. The reason is that when the normal load is just on top of the crack, the crack faces are
push equally against each other closing the crack. When observing the varying magnitude
of shear, it is also noted that for T/P = 1/2, the energy release rate is almost zero for
the entire range of positive µ, i.e. this unique loading situation created conditions where
the loads are cancelling each other in term of the correspond energy release rate. This
study show that under the start ups in a bearing where the oil ﬁlm is not yet established
and a very large shear force exists, signiﬁcant amounts of damage is added to the bearing
compared to the running state at which the shear is very small.
Figure 5.7a presents a study only considering surface shear and crack face traction. It
is observed that the energy release rate is a magnitude larger than the previous analysis
in Figure 5.6, suggesting that the pressurized oil entering the crack is a dominating factor.
For the previous analysis of the load cases, the maximum energy release rate was found
either when the load was acting before or after the crack opening. In Figure 5.7a, the
maximum energy release rate is when the load is positioned on top of the crack (µ = 0).
The explanation for this is that the crack face traction is dominant and since the pressure in
the crack is a function of the pressure just on top of the crack, the maximum contribution
is found when the load is on top of the crack. The crack face traction from the pressure, is
expected to give a large contribution, as the pressure will always open the crack is a Mode-I
type of behaviour, in contrast to the two other loads, which for certain conﬁgurations will
close the crack. By observing the graphs, it is seen that the diﬀerence between the curves
in both behaviour and magnitude is small for the diﬀerence magnitudes of surface shear.
This further proves that the crack face traction term is the most dominant. The surface
shear has a very small contribution as it can be seen that the data point in the positive
range of µ, gives a slightly larger value than in the range of negative µ. This coincide with
the previous analysis showing that the surface shear gives it maximum contribution when
the load is positioned after the crack, where the shear will open the crack. When moving
away from the center of the crack (µ = 0), it is seen that the values are decreasing rapidly.
The reason is again that the pressure in the crack is a function of the pressure on top of the
crack opening. When the load is moving away, the pressure will drop rapidly in the crack,
decreasing the energy release rate. When the load is suﬃciently far away from the crack
opening, the energy release rate will then again be dominated by the surface shear, as the
pressure on the crack faces goes to zero.
Figure 5.7b presents the analysis of the combined loading, including both normal load,
==PAGE:76==

surface shear and crack face traction. When comparing to the analysis in Figure 5.7a, the
shape and tendency is similar, but the magnitude of the energy release rate has decreased.
Since the only diﬀerence is that the normal load has been added to the model compared to
the previous analysis, the decrease can be interpreted as a result of the normal load. The
decrease when applying the normal load to the model can be explained by the nature of
the normal load. From the previous analysis investigating the deformation as a result of
==PAGE:77==
5.1. ELASTIC PARAMETER STUDY
65
the normal load (see Appendix A), it was found that the normal load is actually closing the
crack. The normal load will thereby counteract the oil entering the crack trying to open
the crack and thereby lower the energy release rate. As mentioned, the general tendency is
the same as for the case without normal load (Figure 5.7a), showing that the contribution
from the normal load does not change the general tendency signiﬁcantly.
For the last two studies involving pressure on the crack faces, it was determined that
the dominating factor regarding crack growth is the pressurized oil enter the crack, exerting
a load on the crack faces. The reason is concluded to be a result of that this load type
will always contribute to the opening of the crack. The result of the analysis considering a
pressure on the crack faces could be discussed in the aspect of the oil actually entering the
crack (the two other loads will under all conditions be present). The crack need a certain
size for the oil to be able to enter, giving this damaging contribution. It should also be
noted that even though the oil is able to enter the crack, the crack has to reach a certain
size, for the load exerted to give a signiﬁcant contribution to the crack growth. The reason
for this is that as the crack increases in size, the area of the crack faces increase and thereby
also the area of which the oil pressure can act on. This also indicate that the crack growth
rate will be small at ﬁrst and when a certain size of the crack is reached the oil is able
to enter, increasing the crack growth rare signiﬁcantly. Another aspect is that even when
the crack has a suﬃcient size for the oil to enter the crack, the oil might still be prevented
from entering the crack by other mechanisms. The prevention could be a result of that it
was discovered in this analysis that some load cases actually closing the crack. However
it is diﬃcult to determine under which conditions the oil will enter the crack and give a
contribution. This means that the analysis considering the pressure acting on the crack
faces should be considered as a worst case scenario.
To further investigate the inﬂuence of the crack length, a study of varying crack depth
is conducted. In this study the crack remains perpendicular to the surface and a is varied
according to Figure 5.3a. The center of the transitions zone is kept at x1 = 0 and material
parameters is still based on Eq. (4.1) with γ = 10. The loading scenarios used in the
model, is the combined loading of normal load, surface shear and crack face traction and
a load case with a normal and surface shear. This analysis should investigate the energy
release rate as a function of the crack size, in order to investigate whether a critical crack
size exist, where the crack starts to grow rapidly.
Figure 5.8a presents the study of a varying crack depth for the combined load case.
It is seen that the energy release rate is low in the beginning when the crack is small as
expected. For a large amounts of surface shear, the energy release rate is almost linear until
a/d = 0.8. For low surface shear, a behaviour where the energy release rate is almost zero
for a small cracks is observed. When the crack reach a length of approximately a/d = 0.05,
the energy release rate start to increase signiﬁcantly for T/P = 1/10 and T/P = 0. This
means that the oil pressure is getting its maximum eﬀect when the crack has reached a
length of a/d = 0.05, in this speciﬁc conﬁguration (5% of the layer thickness). The energy
release rate is then increasing almost linearly until a/d = 0.6 is reached. The maximum
of the energy release rate is reached at approximately a/d = 0.8 for all magnitudes of the
shear, and afterwards the energy release rate starts to decrease again. The reason for the
maximum is that, as the crack grows the loads have an increasing eﬀect on the energy release
rate (especially the oil pressure in the crack), but at the some point the crack tip reaches
the transition zone, where the material stiﬀness increases, lowering the energy release rate.
In experiments conducted by Juul [4], it was observed that at some point the crack will
start to grow along the interface rather than continue growing towards the interface. This
is a result of that it at some point requires less energy to grow in this direction.
Figure 5.8b presents the case of only normal load and surface shear applied. It is seen

that for a small shear load, the energy release rate is close to zero. The reason is that the

that the forces exerted on the surface gives a very limited contribution to the stress state

the load positioned at the position of maximum energy release rate according to Figure 5.6b
in order to gain better insight. The model is the same as previously, but the load is now
positioned at µ = −0.8. The result is presented in Figure 5.9. It is observed that compared
to µ = 0, the critical crack depth has increased, to when the crack has grown half way
though the ﬁlm. When comparing the result of T/P = 1 in Figure 5.8b and 5.9, it is seen
that the magnitude at the maximum is only slightly larger. For the two other graphs with
zero or small surface shear, the energy release rate is larger. This is also in compliance with
Figure 5.6b, where it is seen that for µ = 0, the energy release rate is close to zero and for
µ = −0.8 the energy release rate has a signiﬁcant magnitude. Comparing to the case also
including the oil entering the crack, the magnitude is still signiﬁcantly smaller, rendering
the oil in the crack the dominating factor. When observing the energy release rate and
crack depth of the graphs for low and zero shear in Figure 5.9, it is seen that the energy
release rate is small until around a/d = 0.1 and then its start to increase. Compared to
Figure 5.8b, the crack should be approximately twice as large before the loading start to
aﬀect the crack signiﬁcantly.

CHAPTER 5. PARAMETER STUDY OF CRACKS IN HYDRODYNAMIC
BEARINGS
5.2
Parameter Study of Interface-, Deﬂected- and Inclined
Cracks
The ﬁrst parameter study involved edge-cracks growing perpendicular to the bearing sur-
face, which were used for investigating the contribution form the diﬀerent types of load.
In this part, interface cracks, deﬂected and inclined cracks are investigated in order to de-
termine if the same conclusions are found and to investigate if the path of the crack has
an inﬂuence. Interface cracks are of interest (see Figure 5.10), as is it observed in bearings
that cracks can appear along the interface without having started from the surface. The
best explanation for interface cracks to appear is due to poor bonding between the two
materials. It is also observed from experiments that poor bonding leads to failure of the
bearing very rapidly relative to bearings where the bonding is good. As seen from previous
analysis the most damaging factor is the oil pressure acting on the cracks faces, but for an
interface crack, the oil cannot enter the crack leaving only the normal pressure and surface
shear to have an eﬀect. The deﬂected crack (see Figure 5.15) is observed in the bearings
as the cracks grow from the surface to the interface, and then starts to grow along the
interface as it is easier than growing into the much stiﬀer backing material. In this case it
is interesting to investigate which direction the crack is the most probable to proceed in,
when the interface is reached. Cracks are also observed to grow at an angle (see Figure
5.12) relative to the surface. From experiments it is seen that the direction of the crack,
until it reaches the interface can vary. It is investigated whether it is most probable for the
crack to grow at a positive or negative angle.
These studies are performed under the same geometrical, material and load speciﬁca-
tions as in the previous studies.
The ﬁrst study performed, is related to the interface crack. The model is illustrated
in Figure 5.10.
The interface crack has two cracks tips, and the energy release rate is
determined for both crack tips as the load is moving across. The parameter µ describing
the position of the load is now relative to the center of the interface crack (origo in Figure
5.10), as the crack does not have any opening to the surface.
Figure 5.11 presents the results of the interface crack study. In the ﬁgure, the results
are marked “R” and “L” indicating the energy release rate of the right and left crack front,
respectively (relative to ﬁgure 5.10). Investigating Figure 5.11a, which is the case of only
surface shear, it is observed that the maximum for both the left and right crack tip occurs
when the load has passed the respective crack tip as in the study of perpendicular edge-
cracks. It is also observed that before the load passes the center (µ = 0), the locally largest
energy release rate is for the left crack tip, while the largest energy release rate is at the
right crack tip when the load has passed the center. By observing both curves, it is seen

is reduced, the curves become more identical and symmetric around their respective position

The second study concern cracks growing at an angle. The model is illustrated in Figure
5.12. A study in conducted on a structure with a negative and a positive angle (±30◦) in
order to determine the most probable direction of the crack. As the crack has an opening
on the surface, the load situation will involve normal, shear and crack face loads. Figure
5.13 and 5.14 present the results for the diﬀerent load cases. In each plot, the energy release
rate is shown for both a negative and positive angle, for easy comparison, indicted with
±30 at each curve.
Figure 5.13a presents the study for surface shear only. The same behaviour as for the
perpendicular edge-crack is observed, where the maximum energy release rate is found after
the opening is the surface has been passed. This holds for both the negative and positive
angle. It is seen that the positive crack angle gives a signiﬁcantly larger energy release rate
than the negative angle suggesting that cracks with a positive direction (direction of the
shear), is the most probable direction of growth. This is expected as the shear will open
the crack with a positive angle, while it will be closing the crack with a negative angle.
Figure 5.13b presents the study for normal and surface shear. In contrary to the previous
studies, this study show a diﬀerent behaviour. It is observed that now the maximum is
found when the loads has passed the opening, where for the perpendicular edge-crack, the
maximum was located before the load passed the crack opening. It is also observed that
for zero shear, the curves are identical (but with diﬀerent position of maximum), as the
load is symmetric, which makes it equally probable for the crack to grow in either direction.
When the magnitude of the shear is increased, it is observed that the energy release rate for
the crack growing in a negative direction is decreasing while the energy release rate for the
crack growing a the positive direction is increasing. Again the reason is that the shear will
open a crack growing in the positive direction while closing a crack growing in a negative
direction. It is observed that the curve for T/P = 1 for the positive crack, has three peaks
where the most dominant is located at µ = 3.6. For the case of a positively angled crack,

==PAGE:85==

73
at this position. The same tendency as for the perpendicular edge-crack is observed, where
the normal load is actually beneﬁcial, as this load is closing the crack, reducing the energy
release rate. In general when comparing the studies of the angled crack to the studies where
the crack is perpendicular to the surface, it is seen that the energy release rate is larger for
the angled cracks. The reason is that when an angled crack has reached a certain depth,
the crack is longer, giving the most damaging loads in terms of surface shear and crack face
traction, a greater impact. Especially the crack face traction will have an increased eﬀect
as a longer crack gives a larger area for the pressure to act on.
The third study concern deﬂected cracks, where the crack has reached the interface
and then starts to grow along the interface. The model is illustrated in Figure 5.15. Two
studies are conducted where the crack grows to the left and to the right when the interface
is reached, respectively, is order to determine the most probable direction of the crack. As
the crack has an opening to the surface, the loads will again involve normal, shear and
crack face loads. In the initial studies of the deﬂected crack, a problem with the contact
model was encountered. The problem was the contact in the vertical part of the crack. In
this region the contact model applied very large pressures to avoid the surfaces overlapping,
which is a result of mechanical locking mechanisms/friction not being accounted for in the
contact model.
This lead to unrealistic energy release rates and in order to avoid this
problem, it was chosen no to account for contact in the vertical part of the crack.
Figure 5.16 and 5.17 present the results for the diﬀerent load cases. In each plot, the
energy release rate is shown for both the crack growing to the left and the right when the
interface is reached, for easy comparison. This is indicated in the plots with a “R” for right
and a “L” for left, relative to Figure 5.15.
Figure 5.16a presents the study for pure surface shear.
Identical behaviour to the
previous studies is observed, where the maximum energy release rate is located when the
load has passed the crack opening. The physical mechanism is that the crack growing to
the interface and then along the interface creating a “strip”. This strip will be bend up for
a crack growing to the right, opening the crack, and for a crack growing to the left, the
“strip” will be bend down, closing the crack. This will make the crack most probable of
growing to the right.
Figure 5.16b presents the study for normal and surface shear load. The behaviour found
in this study was unexpected as the crack growing to the left seems as likely as the crack
growing to the right due to the maximum in energy release rate being similar in magnitude.
The explanation for this is that the normal load will close the crack for both directions
of growth. As the crack is closed by the normal load, the surface shear (no matter the
direction of the crack) can only give a contribution in terms of stretching or compressing the
“strip”, by sliding the crack faces coinciding with the interface relative to each other. This

corresponds to the mechanism of a Mode-II crack which is identical for both directions of
growth, giving similar energy release rates. It should though be considered that the contact
model does not account for friction. For a crack growing to the left both the shear and
normal load will close the crack, creating a large friction force countering the sliding of the
surfaces (Mode-II behaviour). When the crack grow to the right, the surface shear opens
the crack resulting is less compressive forces, creating a smaller friction force. Again when
shear is not presented in the model, the solutions become identical relative to the crack tip
position rendering the two directions equally probable.

where the crack faces traction has the largest magnitude. The situation where the crack
is growing to the right gives the largest energy release rate when surface shear is present.
For no surface shear, the curves are identical. This again indicate that the crack is likely
to grow in both directions when no shear is present, but it is most probable for the crack
to grow to the right as soon as shear is present.

general tendency is the same as in Figure 5.14b, and as seen before, the normal load is
closing the crack, reducing the energy release rate. By investigating the curve for a crack
growing to the left with T/P = 1, it is observed that the maximum energy release rate
has a slight oﬀset to the positive µ value. This is a result of both the normal and surface
shear load closing the crack for negative µ, and therefore a larger energy release rate is
obtained when the load is moving slightly to the right reducing the load on the “strip” and
thereby the closing of the crack. For lower amounts of shear, the closing of the crack is less
dominant and the maximum is at µ = 0 as previously observed.
In the study of angles and deﬂected crack it was generally found that the crack tends
to grow to the right (the direction of the surface shear), as the crack was opened by both
the shear and crack face load in these situations. Is was also found that for a very small
amount of shear, the crack is equally probable of growing to the left, however this is unlikely
as shear will always be present under start up of an engine where there is direct contact
between the metal surfaces in the bearing. The magnitude of the energy release rate was
also generally larger for these types of cracks, especially when crack face tractions were
included as the length of the crack face is increased giving a larger area for the pressure to
act on.
5.3
Crack Propagation Parameter Study
In this parameter study, the propagation path of cracks are investigated. The study is based
on the interaction integral procedure which is presented in Section 3.4. The interaction
integral procedure is used in order to determine the stress intensity factors KI and KII.
When the stress intensity factors has been determined, the maximum stress criterion shown
in Eq. (3.84) is utilized in order to determine the angle of propagation. For each load
position, the angle of propagation is then determined. In addition to this, the sum of the
squared stress intensity factors, K2
I +K2
II, is also determined for each load position in order
to give a total value of the stress intensity. Then the assumption of that the crack will grow
in the direction of the largest stress intensity factor is used.
When determine the stress intensity factors KI and KII, some cases yields a negative
result. For the Mode-II stress intensity factor KII (the shearing mode) the positive versus
negative values is a matter of the shear direction.
By observing the Mode-II crack in
Figure 3.1, the top can slide either to one or the other side while the bottom slide on the
opposite direction (change sign on force arrows), giving two diﬀerent signs on KII. For the
Mode-I crack, the positive value correspond to the crack being opening, while a negative
value correspond to the crack being closed. This means that a negative KI, value does not
contribute to the crack growth. In the numerics of the program a negative KI is therefore
set equal to zero.
A convergence study has also been conducted on the interaction integral procedure,
however it was chosen not to shown it, since the same conclusion as for the J-integral was
reached. To summarize, the solution is converged even for a small number of elements in
a homogeneous material. For the FGM, it is more diﬃcult to obtained a fully converged
solution as an increased number of elements change the properties of the transition zone.
Again, the same element size is used for all studies, in order to have identical transition
zones (structures), making the solutions comparable.
The model which is used for the crack propagation study is illustrated in Figure 5.18 and
again the loading situation (P = 40) and material parameters are the same as previously
used. As the crack propagation study is based on the interaction integral, an evaluation
of the domain integral is required around the crack tip, on a path which is straight. In

Figure 5.18: Crack propagation model with dimensions W = 12 and d = 2 (distance to transition).
The crack increment for each step is kept constant at ai = 0.4. The dashed line indicate material
transition.
order to accommodate this, the crack is being divided into several straight pieces with a
ﬁnite length. The length of each piece is chosen to remain constant at ai = 0.4. This
length is chosen as it gives a suﬃcient amount of elements on a straight path within the
domain integral to obtain satisfactory results, but also as a compromise to the fact that
each mesh is generated manually in Ansys, making the process very time consuming. The
procedure for the calculations is as follows. First an initial crack angle and increment length
(ai = 0.4) is assumed, which is related to the variables φ1 and a1. Then the stress intensity
factors is determined as a function of the load moving across. Afterwards the largest stress
intensity factors (K2
I + K2
II) are inserted into the maximum stress criterion which provide
the angle in which the crack will proceed. Then a new crack increment, a2 is added to the
model (creating a new mesh again), with an angle φ2 which was just determined, based
on the previous increment. This process in then continued until a satisfactory part of the
crack path is obtained. As mentioned, the ﬁrst part of the path has to be based on an
assumption. In the study of crack propagation, diﬀerent initial crack has been assumed in
order to determine if it has an inﬂuence on the continued crack path. In this study the
assumed initial cracks, is a crack perpendicular to the surface (φ1 = 0◦), and a crack with
a φ1 = ±30◦starting angle, according to Figure 5.18.
The ﬁrst study conducted, is with an initial crack of φ1 = 0◦. The study in conducted
with only surface shear applied. To further show the procedure used in order to determine
the crack path, the graph of the crack angle and the sum of the squared stress intensity
factors has been shown as a function of the load position for the ﬁrst study in Figure 5.19.
Figure 5.19 presents the stress intensity and angle for each step of crack propagation i
(in this case six steps has been conducted). First utilizing the assumption of the crack
growing in the direction of the largest stress intensity, the location in term of load position
is determined from the right plot. The next step to determine the angle at this load position
from the left graph, which is used for the next crack increment. The result of this process is
then presented visually in Figure 5.20. In Figure 5.20 (and for the following visualizations),
the model has been turned 90◦clockwise in relation to Figure 5.18 (direction of surface shear
is from left to right). In the ﬁgures showing the crack path (section of total model), two
zones (layers) is seen which is the top layer/ﬁlm (blue), the bottom layer/substrate (red)
and the transition zone. This shows where the crack is located relative to the interface.

For the other studies conducted, the graphs used for determining the crack propagation
can be found in Appendix C, and only the visual plot of the crack path is presented in the
report.
Figure 5.20 presents the crack path for surface shear. It is seen that the crack never
reaches the interface, but is immediately stating to bend to the right (direction of surface
shear), and will at the current point be very probable of braking oﬀas a ﬂake. In experi-
ments conducted, it is normally seen that the crack grow to the interface, but cases where
ﬂakes break oﬀthe top part of the ﬁlm is also observed. An example of this can be seen in
Figure 5.21, which is a cross sectional cut of a bearing used in the experiments conducted
by Juul [4]. In Figure 5.21, the ﬁlm is in a severely damaged state, but it is observed that
crack growth in the top part of the ﬁlm has lead to material breaking oﬀ.
Figure 5.22 presents the crack path for a case considering normal load. The crack path
is determined for both an initial crack with φ1 = 0◦and φ1 = 30◦. As only a normal load
is acting, the crack path presented in Figure 5.22b for an initial crack of φ1 = 30◦will be
similar to an initial crack of φ1 = −30◦, but with the crack mirrored around a vertical axis.
For the case of a φ1 = 0◦initial crack in Figure 5.22a, the crack is growing to the interface
==PAGE:91==
5.3. CRACK PROPAGATION PARAMETER STUDY
79
Figure 5.21: Cross sectional cut of real bearing showing state where bearing material has broken

in a S-shape. This is a result of the length of the crack increments. For shorter increments
(ai), the same shape would be observed, but with a smaller amplitude, i.e. for inﬁnite
small increments, the crack would be a straight line growing to the interface (experimental
example presented in Figure 5.23). This means that to obtain the actual path, a general
tendency of the path shown should be taken. This is the case for all studies conducted in
this parameter study.
In Figure 5.22b it is observed that the crack is growing in a straight line (as the ten-
dency), but at a slight inclination in the negative direction. The same would be found for
a negative initial crack, but then the crack would have a slightly positive inclination. This
indicate that the initial crack has an inﬂuence on the continued path of the crack, however
the inﬂuence is small in this case.
For both studies (and the following), the crack propagation study has been stopped
when the crack reach the interface. The model did not explicitly predict that the crack
deﬂect and grow along the interface. The reason for this is primarily the mode but also the
magnitude of the crack increment. The crack increments are fairly large as the study would
otherwise be too time consuming as a new mesh needs to be generated manually each time.
However this has a downside, since one increment can bring the crack from a state in the
ﬁlm (relatively homogeneous material point) to the substrate, and the model is therefore
not able to account for the gradual change. The other and more signiﬁcant factor is that
a crack is normally assumed to propagate when the stress intensity reaches a critical level
==PAGE:92==
80
CHAPTER 5. PARAMETER STUDY OF CRACKS IN HYDRODYNAMIC
BEARINGS
Figure 5.23: Cross sectional cut of bearing showing a crack propagating perpendicular to the
surface (direction of shear unknown), Juul [4].
Kc. The critical level Kc will change depending on the location and direction due to the
non-homogeneous material. As the model does not account for this eﬀect, that it is easier
for the crack to grow in certain directions, the deﬂection when the interface is reached
is not predicted. An attempt was made to take the varying Kc into account. This was
done by utilizing the strain energy criterion rather than the maximum stress criterion for
determining the crack angle. The strain energy density criterion is based on the direction
giving the lowest strain energy. By then scaling the stress intensity factors used in the strain
energy criterion by diﬀerent Kc depending on the direction of growth, some direction should
be more favourable. This simple approach did not work as intended as a more advanced
scheme is needed to predict the deﬂection. However from experimental data the tendency
of cracks growing along the interface when it is reached is always observed, making this
part less interesting. From this perspective the cracks in this study should be assumed to
deﬂect and grow along the interface (most likely in direction of the surface shear, as it was
found in the study of deﬂected cracks).
Figure 5.24 presents a study for crack face tractions only. For a φ1 = 0◦initial crack, it
is seen that the crack grow straight down to the interface. This was expected as the model
is symmetric and an equal force is applied on both crack faces creating a pure Mode-I crack.
For the case where the initial crack has an angle of φ1 = 30◦, the crack is bending rapidly to
the right, never growing to the interface. In this case the initial crack has a large inﬂuence

on the continued crack growth, making the crack grow in the direction of the initial crack.
For a negative angle the same eﬀect would be observed, but mirrored in a vertical axis.
Figure 5.25 presents a study for crack face traction and normal load. For a φ1 = 0◦
initial crack, a crack growing straight down to the interface is observed. For the case with
a φ1 = 30◦initial crack it is observed that the crack also grow straight down, but with a
minor inclination. From the previous study of crack face traction alone the crack was found
to grow to the right in a curve and for the normal load the crack was straight with a slight
inclination. However when combining the two types of load, the crack is only showing the
tendency of the normal load indicating that the normal load is dominant with respect to
the angle of propagation. A possible explanation could also be that at the current state
of crack growth the normal load is dominant in terms of direction, but at a later state the
crack face tractions will become dominant again. However even if the crack face traction
becomes dominant, the crack has taken a straight downward direction and the crack face
traction will then only contribute to growth in this direction.
Figure 5.26 presents a study for crack face tractions and surface shear. This study is
conducted with both a signiﬁcant amount of shear (T/P = 1) and minor amount shear
(T/P = 10) in order to investigate the inﬂuence of the shear force. In Figure 5.26a and
5.26b, results is presented for signiﬁcant surface shear with a φ1 = 0◦and φ1 = −30◦
initial crack. It is observed for both cases that the crack bends to the right in a curve shape
(direction of shear). For the φ1 = 0◦initial crack, the crack is growing far from the interface
and at its current state it is likely to break oﬀas a ﬂake, which is also conﬁrmed by a rapid
increase in the stress intensity factor (see Appendix C, Figure C.8). For the φ1 = −30◦
initial crack, the crack is growing to a deeper level close to the interface. Comparing the
curve of the crack in Figure 5.26a and 5.26b it is observed that these curves are similar,
but due to the initial condition, the path is diﬀerent. It should though be noted that the
diﬀerence in path (one grows deeper than the other), might also be a result of the crack
increment length. For a smaller increment, the turning of the crack path could happen over
a shorter distance, resulting in a more similar path of the two cases. As both cracks bend
to the right, the surface shear is dominant to the direction of the crack. However it should
still be considered that that crack face traction is less dominant in the beginning, allowing
the surface shear to be dominant. When the crack face traction is dominant it is observed
from previous studies that the crack tends to follow the general direction (which in this
case has been changed to a right curve by the surface shear).

conducted but with only a minor amount of surface shear (T/P = 10). For the φ1 = 0◦

to the amount of surface shear. In the beginning the crack face traction will contribute to
straight downward crack growth and the shear is contributing to the bending, but as the
crack is bending, the crack face traction will also contribute to the right bend. For the
φ1 = −30◦initial crack with minor surface shear the opposite behaviour is observed. The
crack is growing in a curve to the left. As seen from the study of crack face traction, the
crack face traction makes the crack propagate in the general direction. In this study where
the surface shear is only minor, the crack face traction becomes dominant which results in
the crack growing to the left. This indicate that for this load case with low amounts of
surface shear, the initial direction has inﬂuence on the continued path. A positive initial
crack has not been investigated as it was concluded from these studies that the crack would
grow to the right, adding no additional information.
Figure 5.28 presents the study for surface shear and normal load. Again the study is
conducted for both signiﬁcant and minor amounts of surface shear. For the cases with
signiﬁcant surface shear presented in Figure 5.28a, 5.28b and 5.28c, it is seen that the
general tendency is for the crack to grow to the right. It is observed that the crack grows
towards the interface regardless of the initial crack. For the φ1 = 0◦and φ1 = 30◦initial
crack angles, it is seen that a sudden deﬂect appears when approaching the interface. The
sudden deﬂection is not unrealistic, however it should be remembered that the magnitude
of crack increments are ﬁxed, meaning that the actual deﬂect is probably much smaller in
length. It is observed that all three cracks has an almost identical angle of propagation.

From the previous study of crack face traction and surface shear, the initial crack has an
inﬂuence on the continued path. The diﬀerence could be explained by the fact that the
crack face tractions contribute to the crack growing in the general direction of the crack.
However from the study of the normal load it was found that independently of the initial
crack, the crack always grew almost straight down. The normal load is then contributing
to the same direction for all cases and only the shear has an inﬂuence which is observed
by the growth to the right (direction of shear). Therefore almost identical crack paths is
observed for the diﬀerent initial cracks in this study.
For the studies with minor surface shear applied, presented in Figure 5.28d, 5.28e and
5.28f a quite diﬀerent behaviour of the cracks is observed. For the φ1 = 0◦initial crack
in Figure 5.28d, the minor surface shear applied has changed that path to now being at
an approximately 40◦angle (similar to the case of signiﬁcant surface shear).
However
when inspecting the φ1 = 30◦and φ1 = −30◦initial crack and comparing to the study of
only normal load in Figure 5.22, the paths are identical suggesting that the shear has no
inﬂuence. This was unexpected, but conﬁrm that the initial crack can have a signiﬁcant
inﬂuence on the continued growth for speciﬁc cases.
Figure 5.29 presents the study for the combined load case of surface shear, normal load
and crack face tractions. For the case of signiﬁcant surface shear presented in Figure 5.29a,
5.29b and 5.29c, the same general tendency as for the previous studies is observed where
the shear bends the crack to the right. Depending on the initial crack, the path is located

85
at diﬀerent depths in the ﬁlm. For the φ1 = 30◦initial crack, the bending is increased by
both shear and crack face traction, giving a crack in the top of the ﬁlm. For the φ1 = 0◦,
the crack bends to the right, giving a sightly deeper path. For the φ1 = −30◦initial crack,
the direction is ﬁrst being turned and results in the crack growing to the interface. However
it should again be noted that the diﬀerent depths can be a result of the crack increment
length. In reality, the turn of the direction in the φ1 = 0◦and φ1 = −30◦case, might
happen over a much smaller region and thereby create more similar cracks in the three
cases.
For the cases of only minor shear presented in Figure 5.29d, 5.29e and 5.29f, the be-
haviour is dominated by the normal load and crack face traction. For the φ1 = 0◦case, the
crack is growing almost straight down with only a minor inclination caused by the shear.
For the φ1 = 30◦initial crack, the crack is growing to the right in the top of the ﬁlm, as both
the crack face traction and surface shear contribute to this direction. For the φ1 = −30◦
case, it is seen that the crack almost growth straight down as for the φ1 = 0◦case. The
combination of the normal load which will give a straight downward direction and the sur-
face shear bending the crack to the right is responsible for this direction. The crack face
traction is not able to dominate the direction in the beginning as the crack is short. When
the crack becomes suﬃciently large the crack has obtained a downward direction which the
crack face traction will contribute to this direction as seen in previous cases.
In the studies conducted on crack propagation it was found that the surface shear is
in general the cause of an inclined crack. The cracks tends to grow in the direction of the
shear force. The study of only crack face traction revealed that the crack face traction
would make the crack grow in the direction of the initial crack. From studies involving
crack face tractions and normal load (no or little shear) corresponding to a hydrodynamic
bearing running ideally, the crack always tends to grow straight down. This indicate that
the inclination of a crack can be related to amount of shear i.e. inclined cracks can be
related to high amounts of shear. In case the oil is not able to enter the crack, a situation
of only normal load and shear will be present. For large amounts of shear, the behaviour
is observed to be virtually independent of the initial crack. However for small amounts of
shear, the initial crack has an inﬂuence of the continued growth. This indicate that the
initial crack is important in order to predict the continued growth. The initial crack in
the actual bearing is diﬃcult to predict, as the origin of the initial crack is unknown. The
initial crack could be a result of the casting process or the micro structure, but also a result
related to the processing of the bearing when it is manufactured. In relation to the crack
propagation studies it should also be noted that the ﬁlm is made of white metal/babbitt.
The white metal is a metal matrix composite which consist of small hard crystals dispersed
into a softer metal. As harder particles exist in the ﬁlm, the crack propagation is also
aﬀected by this. If for example the crack grow into an inclusion of hard particles, the crack
is more likely to grow around the inclusion than though, aﬀecting the crack path.
==PAGE:98==
==PAGE:99==
6 Future Work
This chapter contain suggestions to extensions of both the program and the parameter study
in order to obtain a better understanding of the crack phenomena, but also to investigate
possible solutions in terms of improving the bearing (increase life time).
In this project a normal distribution was utilized as a representation of the actual
pressure distribution in a bearing. However this is a simpliﬁcation and depending of the
bearing design and operational conditions, the pressure proﬁle is changing. A study could
be conducted where the numerical model is expanded into making a calculation of the
actual pressure distribution in a bearing. This could give knowledge about the inﬂuence of
the bearing running conditions, as some conditions might prove to be more damaging than
others. By relating the running conditions to the crack growth it would be possible to design
a bearing running under the least damaging circumstances, increasing the bearing life time.
In addition to using a more realistic pressure distribution, a more realistic material could
also be used. In relation to this, inclusions of harder particles could be modelled in the ﬁlm
in order to determine the inﬂuence on these inclusions on the crack phenomena.
The numerical models in this project are all based on a static approach, however the
problem is dynamic. By implementing the dynamics of the problem, new eﬀect might be
discovered giving a better understanding of the crack mechanism. The numerical model
could also be improved by implementing temperature eﬀects (coupled problem), as the
bearing temperature is changing depending on the operational conditions. As the bearing
is a layered material, a diﬀerence in expansion coeﬃcient contribute to the stress state and
thereby to crack growth. Another improvement of the numerical model could be to expand
into 3D as the code is currently in 2D. This might reveal eﬀect which is not predicted by
the 2D model. This will also make it possible to apply a more realistic load scenario on
the bearing. It should be noted that some of the procedures for determining the domain
integral has to be changed when going to 3D.
In this project the elastic-plastic domain integral never came to work. This problem
therefore remains unsolved, and would be of great interest (not only to this project) to put
additional work into, as the problem has not been solved by others either. By a functioning
elastic-plastic domain integral, it would then also be possible to make predictions of the
plasticity eﬀect on the crack phenomena.
In relations to the crack propagation studies, it was found that the magnitude of the
chosen crack increments might have an inﬂuence on the study. The length was chosen as
a compromise, since the remeshing was done manually in Ansys making the process very
time consuming. In order improve the code both with respect to the results and the time, a
remeshing algorithm could be implemented in the program. This would make it possible to
do studies with much smaller steps is an eﬃcient way. Another approach to this could be
to implement a cohesive model instead of the interaction integral. By utilizing the cohesive
model, remeshing could be avoided. In addition to this, the cohesive model would also
be capable of predicting the crack deﬂecting and growing along the interface when it is
reached.
87
==PAGE:100==
88
CHAPTER 6. FUTURE WORK
As knowledge about the crack phenomena has been obtained, the next step is to optimize
the bearing in order to extend the life time. The current program is already capable of doing
initial studies which could extent the life time of the bearing. A suggestion of an initial
study of optimization could include varying the thickness of the ﬁlm material determining
if an optimal thickness exist. Another study could be of the interface, as cracks is seen
to grow to the interface and then along the interface. It could be investigated whether a
diﬀerent bonding material could be used, as a much softer or harder bonding material might
be beneﬁcial. When altering the interface it could also be investigated whether instead of
a two layer design, a multi layer design consisting of diﬀerent material would be beneﬁcial.
==PAGE:101==
7 Concluding Remarks
A ﬁnite element program with the ability to determined energy release rates in functionally
graded materials (FGM) was developed. An additional program was developed determin-
ing stress intensity factors in FGM with the purpose of predicting the crack propagation
path. Both programs were validated against scientiﬁc papers investigating similar problems
yielding satisfactory results for materials with linear elastic material response. A program
determining the energy release rate for a material with elastic-plastic response was also
developed, however this approach never came to work. The reason for the elastic-plastic
program not working was that the term accounting for incremental plasticity introduced
an error around the crack tip, related to the magnitude of the strain energy density gra-
dient. Several approaches to solve the problem was attempted, but the problem remains
unsolved. The numerical programs with linear elastic material response developed were
therefore solely used for analysing the crack phenomena appearing in hydrodynamic bear-
ings. The following conclusions were achieved during the parameter studies of the crack
phenomena.
The ﬁrst studies based on an edge-crack which has grown to the interface, showed that
the most dominating load was the pressure acting on the crack faces. It was also found that
the surface shear was more damaging than the normal load. The most critical situation
with respect to the pressure on the crack faces is when the load is positioned right on top
of the crack opening, as the pressure on the crack faces is largest at this point. For the
normal load and surface shear, the most critical situation was found to be when the load
was about to pass the crack opening. This was unexpected as the normal load solely has
symmetric characteristics in term of energy release rate and the surface shear solely has its
most damaging position when the load has passed the crack. By combining all the load
types, it was found that the pressure acting in the crack faces still was the dominating factor.
Initially all the load types were deemed damaging to some extent, but it was discovered
that the normal load has a positive contribution as it exerts a force on the surface closing
the crack, reducing crack growth. To further investigate the eﬀect of crack face traction, a
study was conducted varying the crack length, since the longer the crack is, the larger the
area is which the pressure can act on. This study showed that for a small crack localized
in the surface, the inﬂuence of the crack face tractions are small (small area for pressure to
act on), but when the crack grow, the energy release rate started to increased rapidly. This
indicate that small cracks can existing in the surface of the bearing for a major part of the
bearings life time, but when the crack reach a certain size, failure will occur rapidly. When
investigating the eﬀect of crack face tractions it is also important to consider when the oil
is actually able to enter the crack, as it was found that some loading situations actually
closed the crack, which might prevent the oil from entering the crack.
The next study conducted was on an interface crack. These crack are often a result of
a poor bonding between the ﬁlm and substrate when the bearing is manufactured. For an
interface crack, two crack tips exist, however there is no opening to the surface meaning
that the oil cannot enter the crack (no crack face traction). From the parameter study
89
==PAGE:102==
90
CHAPTER 7. CONCLUDING REMARKS
of both individual and combined load cases, it was determined that the two crack tips
had approximately the same maximum energy release rate, but at a diﬀerent location with
respect to the load position.
Due to the maximum energy release rate being of equal
magnitude, the crack is equally probable of growing in both direction.
From experiments, crack are occasionally observed to grow at an angle. A parameter
study with a positive (direction of surface shear force) and negative angle of crack growth
was conducted to determine the most probable direction. Based on these parameter studies
it was found that the contribution to the damage has the same characteristics as for the
crack growing straight down i.e. most damaging factor is the pressure acting on the crack
faces. When comparing the magnitude of the energy release rate for the negative vs. positive
growth angle it was found that the crack is most likely to grow in the positive direction
(the direction of the surface shear force). The reason is that for a negative crack angle, the
normal load and surface shear has a closing eﬀect while the crack face traction is opening
the crack. For the positive crack angle, the normal load has a closing eﬀect, but both the
surface shear and crack face traction contribute to opening the crack. However as implied
by this, the determining factor is the surface shear, so for an ideally running bearing with
almost no shear, the negative and positive angled crack is equally probable.
It is observed from experiments that the crack grow to the interface and then deﬂect to
propagate along the interface. A parameter study was conducted to investigate if the crack
is more probable of deﬂecting in a speciﬁc direction. Based on the studies of the energy
release rate is was found that the crack is most probable of growing in the direction of
the surface shear, since both the surface shear and crack face traction contribute to crack
growth in this direction. Again a case with no shear render it equally probable for the crack
to grow in both directions.
Lastly the study of relating load situations to the crack propagation path was conducted.
It was found that the normal load solely gave rice to a crack growing straight down to the
interface independently of the initial crack. The crack face traction contributed to crack
propagation in the general direction of the crack. The surface shear has the eﬀect of bending
the crack in the direction of the surface shear independently of the initial crack direction.
For the case of normal load and crack face traction it was found that the crack would grow
straight down independently of the initial crack indicating that the normal load is dominant
in term of direction. It could however also be a result of that the normal load is dominant
at the initial stage and then at a later stage the crack face traction becomes dominant
again. However even if the crack face traction becomes dominant at a later stage, the crack
has already taken a straight downward direction and the crack face traction will then only
contribute to growth in this direction. For the cases including a combination which contain
surface shear it seen that as the magnitude of shear is increased, the inclination of the
crack increases in the direction of the shear force creating a curved crack. This suggest that
the angle of the crack can be interpreted as the magnitude of shear present under crack
growth. This would indicate that the bearing was not running ideally as the shear under
such conditions should be very low. For a low magnitudes of surface shear, the crack grow
to the interface and from experiments it is known that it will continue to grow along the
interface. However for a large magnitude of shear, the crack will never reach the interface
as it will grow in the top part of the ﬁlm. From the realistic load cases it was therefore
concluded that the crack generally will grow in the direction of the shear force, if shear is
present, and the amount of shear can be connected to the magnitude of the inclination.
Under the study of crack propagation it was also observed that the cracks growing along
the interface at some point had a sudden large increase in stress intensity suggesting that
the crack has reached a point where a part of the ﬁlm will break oﬀas a ﬂake similar to
experimental observations.

D Appendix D
In order to remain on the yield surface, the plastic increment dF has to be equal to zero.
The variation of the yield function in Eq. (3.29) can be determined as shown in Eq. (D.1)
by utilizing the evolution law in Eq. (3.32).

APPENDIX D. APPENDIX D
When investigating the dimensions of the matrix vector product, it is observed that the
plastic strain in Eq. (3.30) has a length of 4. However the constitutive matrix previously
used for the elastic model has the dimension 3x3. The reason is that the third component
now have a contribution in the plastic part. In order to take this into account, the matrix
has been expanded into a 4x4 as shown in Eq. (D.7), where the extra dimension is related
to the 33 component. In this way, the dimension will match and the 33 component is taken
into account in the plastic part.
[C] =
E
(1 −2ν)(1 + ν)




(D.7)
The pseudo code for implementing this algorithm is shown below is the simplest form.
The main change is related to the construction of the stiﬀness matrix and updating of
the stresses.
In the stiﬀness routine the elastic plastic matrix [Cep] is utilized together
with the yielding conditions based on the yield function F. Afterwards, the increment of
displacements is determined based on the load increment. The increment of displacement
is then feed into the stress routine. In the stress routine the total, elastic and plastic strain
are determined, ﬁnding the new stress state. If the yield surface is exceeded, the yield stress
is also updated. This new state is then used for the new load step. It is noted that all
informations is related to the Gauss points.
For load increment n = 1 : nincr do
{∆P}n = {P}final/nincr
Stiﬀness matrix
[K]n
T = [0]
for e = 1 : ne do
[ke]n
T = [0]
for i = 1 : ngauss do
for j = 1 : ngauss do
if F({σ}n−1, {σy}n−1) < 0 then
[ke]n
T = [ke]n
T + WiWjt[B]T [C][B]|[J]|
else if F({σ}n−1, {σy}n−1) ≥0 then
[ke]n
T = [ke]n
T + WiWjt[B]T [Cep({σ}n−1, {σy}n−1)][B]|[J]|
end if
end for
end for
[K]n
T = [K]n
T + [ke]n
T
end for
Calculating change in displacements:
[K]n
T {∆D}n = {∆P}n
{D}n = {∆D}n + {D}n−1
Updating the stresses:
for e = 1 : ne do
for i = 1 : ngauss do

E Appendix E
The numerical procedure presented as pseudo code for the contact model.
Determine facing elements on crack face
Determine distance between center nodes ∆= UB + UA
Predeﬁne PAB = 0
if ∆> ε then
PAB = PAB + ∆P
else if ∆< 0 and PAB > 0 then
PAB = PAB −1
3∆P
end if
if PAB < 0 then
PAB = 0
end if
Reevaluate displacement ﬁeld.
The parameter ε, is the maximum permissible gap, when estimating the surface pressure
under contact and ∆P is a load increment added or subtracted in order to maintain the
surfaces from overlapping which is scaled by the magnitude of the overlap.

F Appendix F
This appendix contain the most important subroutines utilized in the numerical models
presented in Chapter 3.
Basics
• contact - Contact model compensating for overlap.
• cone - Cone used for q-function.
• recover - Linear extrapolation of data from Gauss points to nodes.
